Second Proof: The Argument from Possibility
Presents the second proof from the possibility (imkan) of the world, based on composition and the nature of the necessary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure and reasoning of the proof from possibility (imkan) and how it demonstrates that the world has an efficient cause.

Core Idea
The world's composition proves it is possible, not necessary, and thus needs a cause.

This passage presents the second major proof for the existence of Allah, known as the argument from possibility. It argues that the world, being composite and multiple, cannot be necessary, and therefore must be possible. Every possible being requires an efficient cause. The passage also addresses critical objections, showing the strength of this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the argument's core: 'the world is possible because it is composed'.
2
Identify the key premises
Identify the two main premises: the world is composite and multiple, and the Necessary is simple and one. Understand why these lead to the conclusion that the world is not necessary.
The proof relies on the dichotomy of existence: everything is either necessary or possible.
3
Understand the syllogistic structure
Map out the logical syllogism: Major premise (Necessary is simple), Minor premise (World is composite), Conclusion (World is not necessary, hence possible). Then apply the causality principle: every possible has a cause.
This syllogism is the backbone of the proof.
4
Examine the objections and responses
The passage addresses an objection about circularity and another about whether the cause could be the thing itself. Study how the author defends the proof without assuming the existence of the Necessary.
The objection about circularity is deflected by noting that the premise is conceptual, not existential.
5
Reflect on the cause distinction
Consider the final objection: the proof only shows the world has a cause, but that cause could be its own essence. The author replies by distinguishing between giving existence to oneself versus to another.
This distinction is crucial for establishing an external creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : الاستدلال بإمكانها وهو أن العالم الجوهري ممكن لأنه مركب من الجواهر الفردة إن كان جسما وكثير إن كان جسما أو جوهرا فردا والواجب لا تركيب فيه ولا كثرة بل هو واحد حقيقي وكل ممكن فله علة مؤثرة . قوله : (وكل ممكن يحتاج إلخ ) فيه أنه رجوع إلى الاستدلال بالإمكان وكان الكلام في الاستدلال بالحدوث، لا يقال المراد أن كل ممكن يحتاج إلى المؤثر لحدوثه لأنا نقول إن أراد أنه يحتاج إليه لحدوثه فقط يلزم المصادرة وإن أراد أنه يحتاج إليه لحدوثه وإمكانه رجع إلى الاستدلال بالمجموع، وقد عرفت أن الكلام في الاستدلال بالحدوث وحده فالحق أن يكون مبني الكلام هاهنا على شهادة البداهة . قوله : ( والواجب لا تركيب فيه أي ما يكون واجباً لا يكون مركباً ولا متكثرا وهذا الحكم لا يتوقف على العلم بثبوت الواجب في الواقع حتى يلزم الدور بل يكفيه ملاحظته، ثم هذه المقدمة إشارة إلى كبرى القياس والترتيب هكذا العالم الجوهري مركب وكثير ولا شيء من الواجب بمركب ولا كثير فالعالم الجوهري ليس بواجب ويلزم منه أنه ممكن لإنحصار الموجود فيهما فتوهم الاستدراك والاستطراد من قلة التدبر. قوله : ( وكل ممكن فله علة لإمكانه وحدوثه اللازم له فيما سوى الصفات . فإن قلت : الثابت بهذا البرهان بأن له علة لا أن علة موجودة، وإنما يلزم لو بيّن أن الماهية من حيث هي لا تؤثر مع أن جمهور المتكلمين صرحوا بأن الماهية الواجبية علة لوجوده، قلت : قد سبق أنه فرق بين إعطاء وجود نفسه وإعطاء وجود غيره
The second: inference by its possibility, which is that the substantial world is possible because it is composed of individual atoms if it is a body, and multiple if it is a body or an individual atom, and the Necessary has no composition nor multiplicity but is truly one, and every possible has an efficient cause. His saying: (And every possible needs, etc.) In it is that it is a return to inference by possibility, while the discussion was about inference by temporality. It cannot be said that the meaning is that every possible needs the efficient cause for its temporality, for we say: if he means that it needs it only for its temporality, circularity follows; and if he means that it needs it for its temporality and its possibility, it reverts to inference by the composite, and you have known that the discussion is about inference by temporality alone. So the correct is that the basis of discussion here is on the testimony of intuition. His saying: (And the Necessary has no composition in it) i.e., whatever is Necessary is neither composite nor multiple, and this judgment does not depend on knowledge of the existence of the Necessary in actuality so that circularity would follow; rather, its observation suffices. Then this premise is an indication of the major premise of the syllogism, and the arrangement is thus: the substantial world is composite and multiple, and nothing of the Necessary is composite or multiple, so the substantial world is not Necessary, and from that it follows that it is possible, because the existent is confined to the two, so imagining that there is an exception or digression is due to lack of reflection. His saying: (And every possible has a cause for its possibility and the temporality necessarily concomitant to it, except for attributes.) If you say: What is established by this proof is that it has a cause, not that the cause is existent; and that would follow only if it is shown that the essence as such does not effect, whereas the majority of theologians have stated that the Necessary essence is the cause of its existence. I say: It has preceded that there is a difference between giving its own existence and giving the existence of another.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the existence of the Maker, namely the argument from the possibility (imkan) of the world. It begins by stating that the substantial world is possible because it is composite and multiple, while the Necessary (al-wajib) is simple and one. Therefore the world is not necessary, and every possible being requires an efficient cause. The commentary then addresses an objection: this proof seems to revert to inference by possibility whereas the original discussion was about inference by temporality. The answer explains that if a possible is said to need a cause only for its temporality, that is circular; if for both temporality and possibility, it becomes a composite argument. The correct basis here is intuitive (badaha). Then the commentary clarifies that the premise 'the Necessary has no composition' does not presuppose the existence of the Necessary, avoiding circularity; it is merely a conceptual observation. The syllogism is then laid out: the world is composite and multiple, the Necessary is not, so the world is not necessary, hence possible, and every possible has a cause. Finally, an objection is raised: the proof only shows it has a cause, not that the cause is separate, because the essence itself might be the cause. The reply distinguishes between the essence giving existence to itself and giving existence to another.

Key Terms
إمكان
مركب
كثير
واجب
علة
ممكن
بداهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof from possibility presupposes the existence of the Necessary Being, leading to circularity.
Correction: The premise 'the Necessary has no composition' is a conceptual observation about what the Necessary would be if it exists; it does not require actual knowledge of its existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the substantial world's composition indicate that it is possible rather than necessary?
A) Because composition entails multiplicity, while the Necessary is one and simple.
B) Because composition is a sign of temporal origin.
C) Because all composite things are perceived by the senses.
D) Because the Necessary must be material.

Key Takeaway
The argument from possibility uses the world's composite nature to show it is possible, not necessary, and therefore requires an external cause, without presupposing the existence of that cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your life—your very existence—is composite and contingent. Do you live as if you are self-sufficient, or do you constantly acknowledge that you are held in existence by the Necessary Being? What practical habit can you adopt today to remind yourself of this dependence?
?
The argument distinguishes between giving existence to oneself and giving existence to another. In what areas of your life do you act as if you are the source of your own success, forgetting that every breath is a gift? How can you genuinely attribute your achievements to the Creator?
?
If the world is merely possible and could cease to exist at any moment if not sustained, what does that mean for your attachment to material things? Is there something you are clinging to that you need to release in your heart? What step will you take to detach from it and trust in the Sustainer alone?

Third Proof: Temporality of Accidents and the Embryo
Demonstrates a proof for God from embryonic stages

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof from the temporality of accidents, using the example of human embryonic development to argue for a wise Maker.

Core Idea
Temporal changes in the embryo demand a timeless Wise Maker.

The third proof in this text uses a familiar phenomenon—the transformation of the sperm-drop into a human being—to show that such ordered temporal changes cannot occur without a cause. Observing the embryonic stages, the author argues, points necessarily to a wise Maker. Let's read the passage carefully and unpack its reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the passage in Arabic and then the literal translation to get the overall argument. Notice the key terms for the stages of development.
The passage uses 'مثل ما نشاهد' which is a metaphor for certain knowledge.
2
Identify the logical structure of the proof
Break down the argument: (1) We observe temporal stages in the embryo. (2) These stages require an efficient cause. (3) They cannot occur without an agent, nor can they come from a merely temporal cause (circular). (4) Therefore, they must come from a wise Maker.
The proof relies on the impossibility of uncaused temporal events.
3
Examine the lexical clarifications in the commentary
Understand the definitions: nutfah (semen), alaqah (blood clot), mudghah (chewable lump of flesh). Note the Quranic verse (23:14) and why the author omitted mentioning bones.
The commentary explains that 'observation' refers to certain knowledge, not just sense perception.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that the proof relies on empirical observation alone. The passage clarifies it is a rational argument based on the impossibility of uncaused change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : الاستدلال بحدوث الأعراض إما في الأنفس مثل ما نشاهد من النطفة علقة ثم مضغة ثم لحماً ودماً إذ لا بد لهذه الأحوال الطارئة على النطفة من مؤثر صانع حكيم، لأن حدوث هذه الأطوار لا من فاعل محال، وكذا صدورها عن محدث يلزم المصادرة على المطلوب، وإن أقاموا على استحالة الحدوث اتفاقا دليلا استغنوا بذلك الدليل عن هذا القياس . قوله : ( مثل ما نشاهد من انقلاب النطفة إلخ ) المشاهدة كناية عن العلم اليقيني والنطفة في الأصل الماء القليل يقال هذه نطفة عذبة، أي ماء قليل عذب والمراد هاهنا المني والعلقة قطعة من الدم الغليظ والمضغة من اللحم وغيره قدر ما يمضغ والمراد باللحم في قوله : لحماً ودماً اللحم الذي كسي به العظام على ما نطق به قوله تعالى : { فخلقنا المضغة عظاماً فكسونا العظام لحماً } ثم أنشأناه خلقاً آخر فتبارك الله أحسن الخالقين، ويحتمل أن يكون دخول ثم بالنظر إلى مجموع قوله لحماً ودماً ، والأول أظهر وترك المصنف ذكر العظم لتمام الاستدلال بدونه
The third: inference by the temporality of accidents, either in the souls [i.e., living beings] like what we observe from the sperm-drop: a clot, then a lump of flesh, then flesh and blood; for these states that occur to the sperm-drop must have an efficient cause, a wise maker, because the occurrence of these stages without an agent is impossible, and likewise their issuance from a temporal originator would entail circularity in what is sought. And if they bring a proof for the impossibility of [uncaused] occurrence coincidentally [i.e., without a cause], they dispense with this analogy by that proof. His saying: (like what we observe from the transformation of the sperm-drop, etc.) 'observation' is a metaphor for certain knowledge. 'Nutfah' originally means a small amount of water; it is said 'this is sweet nutfah', i.e., a small amount of sweet water. What is meant here is semen. 'Alaqah' is a piece of thick blood. 'Mudghah' is a piece of flesh etc. the size that can be chewed. What is meant by 'flesh' in his saying 'flesh and blood' is the flesh that clothes the bones, as stated in His saying (Exalted): 'Then We created the lump of flesh into bones, then We clothed the bones with flesh' then We produced it as another creation, so blessed is God, the best of creators. And it is possible that the entry of 'thumma' [then] is with consideration of the entirety of his saying 'flesh and blood'. The first is more apparent. And the author omitted mentioning the bone for the completeness of the inference without it.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the existence of the Maker, based on the temporality of accidents, specifically using the example of the development of the human embryo from sperm-drop (nutfah) through various stages (clot, lump of flesh, then flesh and blood). The argument is that these successive states are obviously temporal and require an efficient cause, a wise maker. The alternative—that they occur without an agent—is impossible. If one claims that they could issue from a temporal originator, that would be circular. The commentator explains that the phrase 'what we observe' refers to certain knowledge, not merely sense perception. He then provides lexical clarifications: nutfah is semen, alaqah is a blood clot, mudghah is a chewable lump of flesh. He quotes Quran 23:14 to connect the stages to the divine creative act. The author (al-Iji) omitted mentioning the bone stage for completeness of the inference without it, meaning the proof does not require every detail.

Key Terms
حدوث الأعراض
نطفة
علقة
مضغة
فاعل
صانع حكيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof relies solely on empirical observation of the embryo's development.
Correction: The passage states that 'observation' is a metaphor for certain knowledge, and the argument is rational: temporal changes must have a cause, and that cause cannot be temporal itself.

Checkpoint
What is the core logical claim of the third proof according to the passage?
A) The development stages of the embryo are temporal and therefore must have a wise maker.
B) The sperm-drop is a mixture of elements that naturally transform.
C) The Quran explicitly states that God creates the embryo.
D) The embryo's development is a miracle that proves prophethood.

Key Takeaway
The ordered temporal changes in the embryo prove the existence of a wise Maker, because uncaused temporal occurrence is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that every stage of your existence—from a drop of fluid to a fully formed human—required a Wise Maker. Have you ever truly considered that your very being is not a product of chance but of intentional creation? What difference should that make in how you live today?
?
If temporal changes like the ones in the embryo cannot happen without a wise agent, then what about the changes in your heart—from ignorance to knowledge, from heedlessness to awareness? Are you seeking the Wise Maker's guidance in those changes, or are you attributing them to your own effort? What step will you take this week to consciously depend on Him?
?
The passage argues that the agent cannot be temporal, otherwise it would lead to circularity. This means your origin is not in a chain of finite causes but in the eternal, wise Creator. How does this reality challenge your attachment to temporary things? Will you realign your priorities to focus on what is eternal?

The Second Argument: Possibility and the Need for a Cause
Al-Jurjani presents the second argument and resolves key objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument for God's existence based on the world's possibility, its syllogistic structure, and how al-Jurjani defends it against objections.

Core Idea
The world is composite, so it is possible; every possible needs a cause.

Al-Jurjani presents the second classical kalam argument: the world is possible because it is composed of parts, and everything possible requires an effecting cause. He then addresses two objections: that the argument reduces to an earlier proof, and that it only shows a cause without proving its existence. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and its defenses.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument statement
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the argument is introduced: the world is possible because it is composite, and the Necessary is simple.
The argument uses the concepts of 'possible' (mumkin) and 'necessary' (wajib).
2
Understand the syllogism
Identify the major premise (the Necessary has no composition), minor premise (the world is composite), and conclusion (the world is not necessary, therefore possible).
Al-Jurjani warns against circularity: the judgment does not depend on knowing the Necessary actually exists.
3
Examine the first objection
Read the part about 'return to inference by possibility'. Understand why the objection is raised and how al-Jurjani resolves it by grounding the argument in intuition (badāha).
4
Analyze the second objection
Consider the objection that the proof only establishes a cause, not that the cause exists. See al-Jurjani's response: the distinction between giving existence to oneself vs. to another.
This clarifies that the necessary essence is a cause for its own existence, not for others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الأول : في إثبات الصانع
الاحتياج مشتركة وأخرى بأن الحادث قد اتصف بالوجود بعد العدم فهو قابل لهما فيكون ممكناً وكل ممكن يحتاج في ترجيح وجوده على علمه إلى مؤثر كما سلف في الأمور العامة ( الثاني ( الاستدلال ) بإمكانها وهو أن العالم) الجوهري ( ممكن لأنه مركب ) من الجواهر الفردة إن كان جسما ( وكثير) إن كان جسماً أو جوهراً فرداً والواجب لا تركيب فيه ولا كثرة بل هو واحد حقيقي ) وكل ممكن فله علة مؤثرة قوله : (وكل ممكن يحتاج إلخ ) فيه أنه رجوع إلى الاستدلال بالإمكان وكان الكلام في الاستدلال بالحدوث، لا يقال المراد أن كل ممكن يحتاج إلى المؤثر لحدوثه لأنا نقول إن أراد أنه يحتاج إليه لحدوثه فقط يلزم المصادرة وإن أراد أنه يحتاج إليه لحدوثه وإمكانه رجع إلى الاستدلال بالمجموع، وقد عرفت أن الكلام في الاستدلال بالحدوث وحده فالحق أن يكون مبني الكلام هاهنا على شهادة البداهة . قوله : ( والواجب لا تركيب فيه أي ما يكون واجباً يكون مركباً ولا متكثرا وهذا الحكم لا يتوقف على العلم بثبوت الواجب في الواقع حتى يلزم الدور بل يكفيه ملاحظته، ثم هذه المقدمة إشارة إلى كبرى القياس والترتيب هكذا العالم الجوهري مركب وكثير ولا شيء من الواجب بمركب ولا كثير فالعالم الجوهري ليس بواجب ويلزم منه أنه ممكن لإنحصار الموجود فيهما فتوهم الاستدراك والاستطراد من قلة التدبر. قوله : ( وكل ممكن فله (علة زي لإمكانه وحدوثه اللازم له فيما سوى الصفات . فإن قلت : الثابت بهذا البرهان بأن له علة لا أن علة موجودة، وإنما يلزم لو بيّن أن الماهية من حيث هي لا تؤثر مع أن جمهور المتكلمين صرحوا بأن الماهية الواجبية علة لوجوده، قلت : قد سبق أنه فرق بين إعطاء وجود نفسه وإعطاء وجود غيره .
The first observation - first aim: On proving the Maker.
The need is common and another [argument] is that the originated thing is characterized by existence after non-existence, so it is receptive to both, thus it is possible, and every possible thing needs for the preponderance of its existence over its non-existence an effecter, as has preceded in the general matters. The second (argument) is the inference by its possibility: that the substantial world is possible because it is composed, from the individual atoms if it is a body, and multiple if it is a body or an individual atom, and the Necessary has no composition and no multiplicity but is truly one, and every possible thing has an effecting cause. His statement: 'And every possible thing needs etc.' [means] in it there is a return to inference by possibility, but the discussion was about inference by occurrence. It cannot be said that what is meant is that every possible thing needs the effecter for its occurrence, for we say: if he means that it needs it for its occurrence only, begging the question follows; and if he means that it needs it for its occurrence and its possibility, then he has returned to inference by the composite, and you know that the discussion is about inference by occurrence alone. So the truth is that the basis of the statement here should be on the testimony of intuition. His statement: 'And the Necessary has no composition' means that whatever is necessary is not composite nor multiple. This judgment does not depend on knowledge of the existence of the Necessary in reality, such that circularity would follow; rather its observation suffices. Then this premise is an indication of the major premise of the syllogism, and the arrangement is thus: the substantial world is composite and multiple, and nothing of the Necessary is composite nor multiple, so the substantial world is not necessary, and from this it follows that it is possible, because the existent is confined to the two [i.e., necessary and possible]. So the suspicion of correction and digression stems from lack of reflection. His statement: 'And every possible thing has a cause' [means] a cause for its possibility and its occurrence necessarily attached to it, besides the attributes. For if you say: what is established by this proof is that it has a cause, not that the cause exists; and that only follows if it is shown that the essence in itself has no effect, while the majority of mutakallimūn have stated that the necessary essence is a cause for its existence. I say: it has preceded that there is a difference between giving its own existence and giving existence to another.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument for the existence of God, the argument from the possibility of the world, along with commentary resolving a key objection. The author, al-Jurjani, first states the argument: Everything that is preceded by non-existence is possible, and every possible needs an effecter (muʼaththir). The world is possible because it is composed (of atoms), so it needs a cause. Moreover, the Necessary (wājib) cannot be composite or multiple; thus the world is not necessary, so it is possible. The commentary then addresses a potential objection: someone might say that the argument reduces to the argument from possibility, but the original discussion was about the argument from occurrence (ḥudūth). Al-Jurjani clarifies that if the opponent means the possible needs a cause only for its occurrence, that begs the question; if for both occurrence and possibility, it becomes the composite argument. He concludes that the proper basis is intuition (badāha). He then explains the syllogistic form: major premise (every necessary is non-composite), minor premise (the world is composite), conclusion (the world is not necessary, hence possible). He resolves a circularity concern by noting that the judgment about the Necessary does not require actual existence. Finally, he addresses the objection that the proof only establishes a cause, not an existent cause, by distinguishing between self-causation and causing another.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is circular because it assumes the existence of the Necessary before proving it.
Correction: Al-Jurjani explains that the judgment about the Necessary's simplicity does not require actual existence; mere conceptual observation suffices.

Checkpoint

A) The world is composed of atoms.

D) The world is possible.

Key Takeaway
The second argument for God's existence proceeds from the world's composition to its possibility, then to the necessity of a cause, and survives objections through careful logical distinctions.

?
Al-Jurjani says the principle 'every possible needs a cause' is grounded in intuition (badāha). Have you ever sat in silence and let that intuition wash over you? Or do you usually run from the vulnerability of admitting you are not self-sufficient? What would it cost you to live today from that intuitive certainty?
?
The Necessary has no composition, no multiplicity—He is utterly simple. You are not. Does that contrast pull your heart toward humility or toward self-aggrandizement? What is one concrete action you can take before sunset to act as if your existence is a borrowed gift, not a right?

The Fourth Argument: Proof from the Possibility of Accidents
Al-Jurjani presents the fourth argument based on the possibility of accidents and the need for a specifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth rational argument for God's existence based on the possibility of accidents and the need for an external specifier.

Core Idea
Since bodies are essentially similar, their differing attributes require a specifier outside themselves.

Al-Jurjani now presents another argument for God's existence that draws on the nature of bodies and their accidents. By examining the fact that all bodies share the same essence yet have distinct properties, the argument shows that these differences cannot arise from the bodies themselves but must come from an external source — the Creator. This argument is attributed to Prophet Moses (peace be upon him) and is grounded in a key Quranic verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Get a feel for the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the premises
The argument has two main premises: bodies are similar in essence, and each body has specific attributes. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the conclusion
The necessity of an external specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) is the conclusion. Ask yourself: why must the specifier be external?
4
Reflect on the Quranic reference
Note the verse from Surah Ta-Ha (20:50) quoted by Moses. How does this verse relate to the logical argument?
The verse shows that God gives each thing its specific form.

(الرابع) الإستدلال (بإمكان الأعراض ) مقيسة إلى محالها كما استدل به موسی علیه السلام حيث قال ربنا الذي أعطى كل شيء خلقه ثم هدى . أي أعطى صورته الخاصة وشكله المعين المطابقين للحكمة والمنفعة المنوطة به ، ( وهو أن الأجسام متماثلة ) متفقة الحقيقة لتركبها من الجواهر المتجانسة على ما عرفت (فاختصاص كل من الأجسام (بما له الصفات جائز فلا بد في التخصيص من مخصص له
قوله : (مقيسة إلى محالها لا شك أنه يمكن الاستدلال بإمكان وجوده في نفسه عن خفاء فلم يلتفت إليه . قوله : ( إن الأجسام (متماثلة فلا يكون اختصاص كل جسم بماله غير احتياج إلى مخصص خارجي . من الصفات لذاته من
The fourth is the inference by the possibility of accidents, measured against their loci, as was argued by Moses, peace be upon him, when he said: 'Our Lord is He who gave everything its creation, then guided.' That is, He gave its specific form and determined shape that are in accordance with the wisdom and benefit attached to it. (And it is that) bodies are similar, agreeing in reality because they are composed of homogeneous atoms as you have known, so the specification of each body with its own properties is permissible, so there must be a specifier for the specification. His statement: 'Measured against their loci' – there is no doubt that one can argue by the possibility of its existence in itself from concealment, so he did not pay attention to it. His statement: 'That bodies are similar' – so the specification of each body with what it has cannot be without need for an external specifier. From the attributes of itself from [?].
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument for the existence of God, the argument from the possibility of accidents (imkān al-aʿrāḍ). The argument is based on the similarity of bodies in their essence (since all are composed of homogeneous atoms), yet each body has specific attributes (e.g., shape, color) that are not necessary to its essence. Therefore, the specification of each body with its particular attributes requires a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) external to the body. The author cites the Quranic verse where Moses says: 'Our Lord is He who gave everything its creation, then guided' (20:50), meaning God gave each thing its specific form and shape in accordance with wisdom. The commentary clarifies that the argument is 'measured against their loci' (muqīsatan ilā maḥallihā), meaning the accidents are considered in relation to their subjects. The commentary also notes that one could argue from the possibility of existence itself, but this is less obvious and was not pursued. The second commentary emphasizes that since bodies are similar, the differentiation of their attributes cannot occur without an external specifier.

The Final Argument for a Necessary Existent
Al-Jurjani presents the final dilemma and its conclusion, with a note on reduction to possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conclusive argument for the necessity of the world's director and how it reduces to the argument from possibility.

Core Idea
All proofs for God culminate in the simple idea of possibility.

In this concluding section, al-Jurjani synthesizes the previous arguments into a sharp dilemma: the world's director either is necessary or requires an infinite chain ending in a necessary being. He also reveals that this entire line of reasoning is essentially a restatement of the argument from possibility. This lesson unpacks that final proof and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the core argument
Read the passage that sets up the dilemma for the director of the world and its resolution.
2
Identify the two possibilities
Note how the argument works by considering these two branches.
The argument considers two logical possibilities: necessity or possibility.
3
Understand the rejection of circularity and regress
The teacher explains why circularity and infinite regress are invalid, leading to the necessary existent.
4
See the reduction to possibility
Notice the remark that all this is a lengthening and ultimately returns to possibility alone.
This shows the primacy of the possibility argument in kalam.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this reduction matters: it unifies all arguments under one foundational concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم بعد هذه الوجوه الأربعة نقول مدير العالم إن كان واجب الوجود فهو المطلوب وإلا كان ممكناً فله مؤثر ويعود الكلام فيه ويلزم إما الدور أو التسلسل، وأما الإنتهاء إلى مؤثر واجب الوجود لذاته والأول بقسميه باطل لما مر في مرصد العلة والمعلول من الأمور العامة ( فتعين الثاني وهو المطلوب ) ، ولا يذهب عليك أن ما ذكره تطويل ورجوع بالأخرة إلى اعتبار الإمكان ،وحده والاستدلال به والمشهور أن المتكلمين استدلوا بأحوال خصوصيات من
قوله : ( عن مؤثر لا شعور (له يعني القوة المولدة المذكورة في النطفة كما يدعيه قوم تاهوا في الظلمات واعلم أن الظاهر أن المراد بالأعراض الحادثة هاهنا ليس مجرد كون النطفة علقة وكونها مضغة مثلاً كما يشعر به ظاهر السياق، إذ الظاهر أن هذا الكون اعتباري متجدد ليس بحادث بالمعنى المتعارف بل ما يتفرع على هذه الإنقلابات من الأعراض المختصة بكل منها مثل اللون المخصوص وغيره والله أعلم .
قوله : ( فتعين (الثاني) وهو المطلوب إذ المطلوب هههنا مجرد إثبات واجب الوجوب، أما أن الأشياء بأسرها مستندة إليه ابتداء بلا توسط بعضها في بعض، فذلك بحث آخر مستدل عليه بدليل مذكور في غير هذا الموضع. قوله : ( ورجوع بالآخرة إلى اعتبار الإمكان وحده فإن قلت فليكن معنى قوله وإن كان ممكناً فله مؤثران له مؤثر لحدوثه لا لإمكانه ،وحده حتى يرد ما ذكره قلت مقابلة الممكن بالواجب يمنعه لأن انتفاء الوجوب لا يستلزم الحدوث كالصفات القديمة فإن قلت مدير العالم لا يكون ممكناً قائماً بغيره والممكن القائم بنفسه حادث عند المتكلمين فيصح ذلك التوجيه قلت الملحوظ هاهنا عنوان الوجوب والإمكان لا القيام بنفسه، والقيام بغيره وإن كان الوجوب مستلزماً للقيام بنفسه على أن عدم كون القائم بغيره مدبرا للعالم ليس لاستلزامه الحدوث
Then after these four arguments we say: the director of the world, if he is necessary of existence, then he is what is sought; otherwise he is possible, so he has an effecter, and the discussion returns to him, and either circularity or infinite regress follows. As for ending with an effecter necessary of existence in itself, the first [i.e., circularity] in both its divisions is invalid due to what has passed in the observation of cause and effect from the general matters. So the second [i.e., an infinite chain ending in a necessary existent] is determined, and it is what is sought. And let it not escape you that what he mentioned is a lengthening and a return in the end to the consideration of possibility alone and inference by it. And it is well-known that the mutakallimūn argued by the states of particularities from
His statement: 'About an effecter without consciousness' means the generative force mentioned in the sperm, as claimed by a people lost in darkness. And know that what is apparently meant by 'occurring accidents' here is not merely the [states of] being a clot and being a lump of flesh, as the apparent context suggests, for it is apparent that this [being] is a conceptual renewed [state] not an accident in the conventional sense, but rather what branches from these transformations of the accidents specific to each, like the particular color and other things. And God knows best.
His statement: 'So the second is determined, and it is what is sought' means that what is sought here is merely the proof of the necessary of existence. As for the [claim that] all things are dependent on Him directly without mediation of some by others, that is another research proven by a proof mentioned elsewhere. His statement: 'And a return in the end to the consideration of possibility alone' – if you say: then let the meaning of his statement 'and if it is possible then it has an effecter' be that it has an effecter for its occurrence, not for its possibility alone, so that what he mentioned would be rebutted. I say: the opposition of the possible to the necessary prevents that, because the negation of necessity does not entail occurrence, like the eternal attributes. For if you say: the director of the world cannot be a possible existing by another, and the possible existing by itself is originated according to the mutakallimūn, so that interpretation is valid. I say: what is considered here is the category of necessity and possibility, not existence by itself. And existence by another, even if necessity entails existence by itself, the fact that what exists by another is not the director of the world is not because it entails occurrence.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is a concluding argument that synthesizes the previous four arguments and demonstrates that the director of the world (mudabbir al-ʿālam) must be a necessary existent (wājib al-wujūd). The structure is: The director is either necessary or possible. If necessary, the goal is achieved. If possible, it requires an effecter, leading to either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul), both invalid. Thus we must end with a necessary existent. The commentary notes that this is a 'lengthening' (taṭwīl) because all arguments ultimately reduce to the argument from possibility (imkān). The commentator then addresses a series of objections and clarifications. First, he discusses the phrase 'effecter without consciousness' in the context of the sperm example, clarifying that the 'occurring accidents' (al-aʿrāḍ al-ḥāditha) are not merely the conceptual changes but the specific accidental properties (like color) that result from the transformations. Second, he explains that the proof only aims to establish the existence of a necessary being, not that all things are directly dependent on Him without intermediaries (that is a separate proof). Third, he deals with a potential objection: if the director is possible, maybe the effecter is for its occurrence (ḥudūth) rather than its possibility. The reply is that the lack of necessity does not imply occurrence, as eternal attributes are not necessary yet not originated. Another objection: the director cannot be a possible existing by another (qāʾim bi-ghayrihi) because such entities are originated. The reply: the relevant category is necessity/possibility, not self-subsistence. Even if necessity entails self-subsistence, the fact that a being existing by another is not the director is not due to its being originated.

مدير العالم
واجب الوجود
دور
تسلسل
إمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument for a necessary existent is a separate proof from the argument from possibility.
Correction: The commentator explicitly states that this entire argument is a lengthening and return to the consideration of possibility alone, showing that all proofs reduce to it.

Checkpoint
What is the final conclusion about the director of the world according to this passage?
A) The director is possible of existence.
B) The director is necessary of existence.
C) The director is unknown.
D) The director is a contingent being.

Key Takeaway
The director of the world must be a necessary being, and this conclusion ultimately relies solely on the concept of possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the entire proof for God's existence reduces to the fact of your own contingency, do you honestly live each day aware that you depend on Him at every moment, or do you slip into a sense of independence?
?
The director of the world must be necessary. Does this truth guide your decisions today, or do you still act as if you are the director of your own life?
?
Knowing that infinite regress is not invalid but must end in a Necessary Being, does this make you ponder the weight of your choices? They too must derive their ultimate meaning from the Necessary Existent.

Clarifications on the Path of Possibility
Al-Jurjani provides detailed clarifications on the example and defends the proof from objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the clarifications on the possibility argument, including the nature of the generative force, the meaning of occurring accidents, and the responses to key objections.

Core Idea
The proof from possibility rests on possibility itself, not on occurrence or self-subsistence.

After presenting the core argument, al-Jurjani addresses several points of confusion. He clarifies the example of an 'effecter without consciousness' and the true meaning of 'occurring accidents' in this context. He then defends the proof against objections that might confuse possibility with occurrence or with self-subsistence. This lesson works through these important clarifications.

Guided Path
1
Examine the example of the sperm
Read the commentary on the 'effecter without consciousness' and the generative force in the sperm.
2
Understand the nature of occurring accidents
Note that the accidents here are not the mere conceptual changes but the specific properties resulting from transformations.
3
Review the scope of the proof
See the clarification that the proof only aims to establish a necessary existent, not direct dependence of all things.
4
Follow the objection on possibility vs occurrence
Read the objection that the effecter might be for occurrence, not possibility, and the teacher's response.
5
Understand the objection on self-subsistence
Note the objection about the director not being possible existing by another, and the teacher's distinction between categories of necessity/possibility and subsistence.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم بعد هذه الوجوه الأربعة نقول مدير العالم إن كان واجب الوجود فهو المطلوب وإلا كان ممكناً فله مؤثر ويعود الكلام فيه ويلزم إما الدور أو التسلسل، وأما الإنتهاء إلى مؤثر واجب الوجود لذاته والأول بقسميه باطل لما مر في مرصد العلة والمعلول من الأمور العامة ( فتعين الثاني وهو المطلوب ) ، ولا يذهب عليك أن ما ذكره تطويل ورجوع بالأخرة إلى اعتبار الإمكان ،وحده والاستدلال به والمشهور أن المتكلمين استدلوا بأحوال خصوصيات من
قوله : ( عن مؤثر لا شعور (له يعني القوة المولدة المذكورة في النطفة كما يدعيه قوم تاهوا في الظلمات واعلم أن الظاهر أن المراد بالأعراض الحادثة هاهنا ليس مجرد كون النطفة علقة وكونها مضغة مثلاً كما يشعر به ظاهر السياق، إذ الظاهر أن هذا الكون اعتباري متجدد ليس بحادث بالمعنى المتعارف بل ما يتفرع على هذه الإنقلابات من الأعراض المختصة بكل منها مثل اللون المخصوص وغيره والله أعلم .
قوله : ( فتعين (الثاني) وهو المطلوب إذ المطلوب هههنا مجرد إثبات واجب الوجوب، أما أن الأشياء بأسرها مستندة إليه ابتداء بلا توسط بعضها في بعض، فذلك بحث آخر مستدل عليه بدليل مذكور في غير هذا الموضع. قوله : ( ورجوع بالآخرة إلى اعتبار الإمكان وحده فإن قلت فليكن معنى قوله وإن كان ممكناً فله مؤثران له مؤثر لحدوثه لا لإمكانه ،وحده حتى يرد ما ذكره قلت مقابلة الممكن بالواجب يمنعه لأن انتفاء الوجوب لا يستلزم الحدوث كالصفات القديمة فإن قلت مدير العالم لا يكون ممكناً قائماً بغيره والممكن القائم بنفسه حادث عند المتكلمين فيصح ذلك التوجيه قلت الملحوظ هاهنا عنوان الوجوب والإمكان لا القيام بنفسه، والقيام بغيره وإن كان الوجوب مستلزماً للقيام بنفسه على أن عدم كون القائم بغيره مدبرا للعالم ليس لاستلزامه الحدوث
Then after these four arguments we say: the director of the world, if he is necessary of existence, then he is what is sought; otherwise he is possible, so he has an effecter, and the discussion returns to him, and either circularity or infinite regress follows. As for ending with an effecter necessary of existence in itself, the first [i.e., circularity] in both its divisions is invalid due to what has passed in the observation of cause and effect from the general matters. So the second [i.e., an infinite chain ending in a necessary existent] is determined, and it is what is sought. And let it not escape you that what he mentioned is a lengthening and a return in the end to the consideration of possibility alone and inference by it. And it is well-known that the mutakallimūn argued by the states of particularities from
His statement: 'About an effecter without consciousness' means the generative force mentioned in the sperm, as claimed by a people lost in darkness. And know that what is apparently meant by 'occurring accidents' here is not merely the [states of] being a clot and being a lump of flesh, as the apparent context suggests, for it is apparent that this [being] is a conceptual renewed [state] not an accident in the conventional sense, but rather what branches from these transformations of the accidents specific to each, like the particular color and other things. And God knows best.
His statement: 'So the second is determined, and it is what is sought' means that what is sought here is merely the proof of the necessary of existence. As for the [claim that] all things are dependent on Him directly without mediation of some by others, that is another research proven by a proof mentioned elsewhere. His statement: 'And a return in the end to the consideration of possibility alone' – if you say: then let the meaning of his statement 'and if it is possible then it has an effecter' be that it has an effecter for its occurrence, not for its possibility alone, so that what he mentioned would be rebutted. I say: the opposition of the possible to the necessary prevents that, because the negation of necessity does not entail occurrence, like the eternal attributes. For if you say: the director of the world cannot be a possible existing by another, and the possible existing by itself is originated according to the mutakallimūn, so that interpretation is valid. I say: what is considered here is the category of necessity and possibility, not existence by itself. And existence by another, even if necessity entails existence by itself, the fact that what exists by another is not the director of the world is not because it entails occurrence.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is a concluding argument that synthesizes the previous four arguments and demonstrates that the director of the world (mudabbir al-ʿālam) must be a necessary existent (wājib al-wujūd). The structure is: The director is either necessary or possible. If necessary, the goal is achieved. If possible, it requires an effecter, leading to either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul), both invalid. Thus we must end with a necessary existent. The commentary notes that this is a 'lengthening' (taṭwīl) because all arguments ultimately reduce to the argument from possibility (imkān). The commentator then addresses a series of objections and clarifications. First, he discusses the phrase 'effecter without consciousness' in the context of the sperm example, clarifying that the 'occurring accidents' (al-aʿrāḍ al-ḥāditha) are not merely the conceptual changes but the specific accidental properties (like color) that result from the transformations. Second, he explains that the proof only aims to establish the existence of a necessary being, not that all things are directly dependent on Him without intermediaries (that is a separate proof). Third, he deals with a potential objection: if the director is possible, maybe the effecter is for its occurrence (ḥudūth) rather than its possibility. The reply is that the lack of necessity does not imply occurrence, as eternal attributes are not necessary yet not originated. Another objection: the director cannot be a possible existing by another (qāʾim bi-ghayrihi) because such entities are originated. The reply: the relevant category is necessity/possibility, not self-subsistence. Even if necessity entails self-subsistence, the fact that a being existing by another is not the director is not due to its being originated.

Misconception: The proof from possibility depends on the occurrence (ḥudūth) of the possible.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that the effecter is for possibility itself, not occurrence; lack of necessity does not imply occurrence.

D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
The proof for a necessary existent relies solely on the notion of possibility, not on occurrence or self-subsistence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The proof relies on possibility alone, not on the fact that things occur. When you see the world change, does your certainty of Allah's existence waver? If so, you have attached your faith to the wrong foundation. Rest it on the sheer contingency of everything—including yourself.
?
You claim to believe in Allah as the Necessary Existent. But do your choices reflect that you truly depend on Him for every moment, or do you live in forgetfulness? Let the logical necessity of this proof translate into a heartfelt dependence that transforms how you start your day.

The Fourth Way: Inference from Possibility of Accidents
Presents the fourth way with Quranic evidence and logical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth way of proving God's existence by showing that the specific attributes of bodies require an external specifier.

Core Idea
Bodies are similar in essence; their differences demand a specifier.

This lesson examines the fourth proof from al-Mawaqif, which builds on the idea that all bodies are composed of the same substance yet have distinct attributes. The argument uses the example of Prophet Moses to illustrate that this specification cannot come from the bodies themselves, pointing to an external cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage below along with its translation. Note the structure: the fourth way is introduced, then an example from the Quran, then the logical reasoning.
The passage is concise—take your time with each phrase.
2
Identify the key premises
Look for the claims: (1) bodies are similar in reality, (2) specification of each body with its attributes is possible, (3) therefore a specifier is necessary.
Focus on the logical flow from similarity to the need for a specifier.
3
Connect to the example of Moses
Reflect on the Quranic verse cited: 'Our Lord is He who gave everything its creation, then guided.' How does this illustrate the inference?
The verse shows that each thing receives a specific form and guidance from God.
4
Check your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasp the core of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الرابع) الإستدلال (بإمكان الأعراض ) مقيسة إلى محالها كما استدل به موسی علیه السلام حيث قال ربنا الذي أعطى كل شيء خلقه ثم هدى . أي أعطى صورته الخاصة وشكله المعين المطابقين للحكمة والمنفعة المنوطة به ، ( وهو أن الأجسام متماثلة ) متفقة الحقيقة لتركبها من الجواهر المتجانسة على ما عرفت (فاختصاص كل من الأجسام (بما له الصفات جائز فلا بد في التخصيص من مخصص له ثم بعد هذه الوجوه الأربعة نقول مدير العالم إن كان واجب الوجود فهو المطلوب وإلا كان ممكناً فله مؤثر ويعود الكلام فيه ويلزم إما الدور أو التسلسل، وأما الإنتهاء إلى مؤثر واجب الوجود لذاته والأول بقسميه باطل لما مر في مرصد العلة والمعلول من الأمور العامة ( فتعين الثاني وهو المطلوب )
(The fourth) inference (by the possibility of accidents) measured to their loci, as Moses, peace be upon him, inferred when he said: 'Our Lord is He Who gave everything its creation, then guided.' i.e., He gave its specific form and determined shape corresponding to the wisdom and benefit attached to it. (And that is that bodies are similar) agreeing in reality because they are composed of homogeneous atoms (jawāhir mutajānisa) as you have known. (So the specification of each of the bodies (with the attributes it has is possible, so a specifier (mukhassis) must exist) for that specification. Then after these four ways we say: the director of the world, if He is necessary of existence (wājib al-wujūd), then He is the sought; otherwise, He is possible (mumkin), so He has an effecter (mu'aththir), and the discussion returns upon it, and either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul) follows. But ending with an effecter that is necessary of existence in itself, the two former (types) are invalid due to what has passed in the Observatory of Cause and Effect regarding general matters. (So the second is determined, and it is the sought).
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth way of proving the existence of God and then combines all four ways into a general argument. It begins by stating the fourth way: inference from the possibility of accidents (a'rāḍ) measured to their substrata, citing the Quranic verse from Moses (Quran 20:50). The verse is interpreted as showing that God gave each thing its specific form and shape suited to its wisdom and benefit. Then it states that bodies are similar in reality, composed of homogeneous atoms (jawāhir), so the specification of each body with its particular attributes cannot be due to itself and requires a specifier (mukhassis) external to it. After mentioning these four ways, the text presents a combined argument: the director of the world (mudabbir al-'ālam) is either necessary of existence (wājib al-wujūd) or possible (mumkin). If necessary, that is the sought (al-maṭlūb). If possible, it needs an effecter (mu'aththir), and the discussion would apply to that effecter as well, leading either to circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul), both invalid. The only valid alternative is ending with an effecter that is necessary in itself, which is the sought. The text notes that the two former possibilities are invalid based on earlier discussions in the 'Observatory of Cause and Effect' regarding general principles. Thus the second option (ending with a necessary being) is determined. The author is presenting a concise modal argument that integrates the earlier proofs into a clear disjunctive syllogism.

Key Terms
إمكان الأعراض
الأجسام متماثلة
التخصيص
مخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the fourth way is a design argument based on complexity or fine-tuning.
Correction: The fourth way is actually based on the similarity of bodies and the need for a specifier to explain why similar substances have different attributes. It is not about complexity but about the possibility of specification within a homogeneous substratum.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the fourth way?
A) Bodies are composed of atoms.
B) There must be a specifier for the attributes of bodies.
C) Moses proved God's existence through miracles.
D) Accidents are eternal.

Key Takeaway
The fourth way shows that the specific attributes of bodies, given their essential similarity, require an external specifier, pointing to a Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the unique shape of your body, the specific talents you have, and the exact circumstances of your life, do you truly believe each detail was intentionally specified by a Wise Creator? Or do you live as if these are random? How would your day change if you actively recognized a mukhassis in every detail?
?
The argument shows that bodies are essentially similar, yet each has distinct attributes. Your soul is not a body, but you have a unique personality and journey. If the diversity of bodies requires a specifier, what does the uniqueness of your soul imply about the One who gave it form? Will you thank the Specifier today for the specific way He created you?
?
Moses (AS) used this argument to call Pharaoh to faith. You have just encountered this same argument. If you truly internalized that every specific attribute of yours was given by a mukhassis, would you still complain about your lot? What would it mean to accept your 'specification' as the best possible for you from the All-Wise?

The Combined Argument: From Possible to Necessary Being
Presents a combined modal argument that concludes the existence of a necessary being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the combined argument uses a disjunctive syllogism to prove that the director of the world must be a necessary being, avoiding circularity and infinite regress.

Core Idea
The world's director is either necessary or possible; only necessary ends the regress.

After presenting four ways, al-Mawaqif now consolidates them into a powerful modal argument. The text presents a dilemma: the director of the world (God) is either necessary in Himself or possible. If possible, it would require an external cause, leading to an infinite chain or circularity, both absurd. Thus we must end with a necessary being. This argument integrates the earlier proofs into a clear logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the combined argument passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the disjunctive structure: either necessary or possible, and the elimination of the possible case.
The argument is only a few sentences but packed with logical steps.
2
Analyze the disjunction
Identify the two options for the director of the world: necessary or possible. Understand what each means.
Necessary (wājib) exists by itself; possible (mumkin) needs a cause.
3
Understand why the possible leads to regress
If the director is possible, it needs an effecter (mu'aththir). That effecter would then be subject to the same question, leading to either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul), both invalid.
Recall the earlier discussion on cause and effect that rejects circularity and infinite regress.
4
Conclude with the necessary being
The only remaining option is that the director is necessary in itself. This is the sought conclusion (al-maṭlūb).

Source Text

The Philosophers' Second Path: Proof from Existence
Philosophers' proof from the mere existence of something

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' second path for proving the necessary being, which starts from the undeniable fact that something exists without requiring detailed proofs of creation.

Core Idea
Existence itself is enough to prove God—if not necessary, it leads to Him.

The philosophers propose a simpler yet profound proof: begin with the obvious fact that something exists, regardless of its details. From this single premise, the argument unfolds elegantly: either that being is necessary (which is what we seek) or it is possible and thus requires a necessary cause, avoiding vicious circularity or infinite regress. This path bypasses the complex discussions of the world's temporal origination, offering a more direct route.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
2
Identify the starting premise
The argument begins by asserting that something exists in reality, ignoring all specific details. This premise is attested by innate disposition (fiṭra).
3
Follow the two branches
If the existing being is necessary, we have reached the goal. If it is possible, it needs an effecter and must eventually terminate in a necessary being to avoid circularity or infinite regress.
4
Notice the advantage
The text emphasizes that this path dismisses many burdens of the first path, such as proving the world's temporal origination (ḥudūth) and possibility, along with associated questions and answers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الثاني : للحكماء وهو أن ) في الواقع (موجوداً) مع قطع النظر عن خصوصيات الموجودات وأحوالها وهذه مقدمة يشهد بها كل ،فطرة ( فإن كان ذلك الموجود ) واجباً فذاك ) هو المطلوب ( وإن كان ممكناً احتاج إلى مؤثر ولا بد من الانتهاء إلى الواجب وإلا لزم الدور أو التسلسل وفي هذا المسلك ( طرح لمؤنات كثيرة ) كانت في المسلك الأول من بيان حدوث العالم وإمكانه وما يتوجه عليه من الأسئلة والأجوبة عنها فإنها سقطت هاهنا ( كما ترى .
The second path: For the philosophers, and it is that (in reality (exists) a being) disregarding the specificities of beings and their states. And this is an introduction attested by every innate disposition (fiṭra). (So if that being) is necessary, then that (is the sought). (And if it is possible, it needs an effecter, and there must be an end to the necessary, otherwise circularity or infinite regress follows). And in this path (there is a dismissal of many burdens) that were in the first path of demonstrating the temporal origination (ḥudūth) of the world and its possibility, and the questions and answers directed to it, for they fall away here (as you see).
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage introduces the second path (maslak) for proving the existence of God, attributed to the philosophers (al-ḥukamā'). The argument is concise: it starts from the undeniable fact that something exists in reality (fī al-wāqiʿ mawjūd), without considering the specific characteristics or states of particular beings. This premise is so basic that it is attested by every innate disposition (fiṭra). If that something is necessary of existence (wājib), then it is the sought (al-maṭlūb). If it is possible (mumkin), it needs an effecter (mu'aththir), and we must eventually reach a necessary being to avoid circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul). The advantage of this path is that it sheds the many burdens (mu'anāt) of the first path, which required proving the temporal origination (ḥudūth) of the world and its possibility, along with the associated questions and answers. Those complexities are bypassed here. The author presents this as a simpler, more elegant proof.

Key Terms
المسلك الثاني
الحكماء
فطرة
واجب

مؤثر
دور
تسلسل
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This path is the same as the proof from contingency (imkān) which requires detailed analysis of the world's possibility.
Correction: This path is more fundamental: it starts from the mere fact of existence, not from the world's contingency. It bypasses the need for proving the world's temporal origination or possibility.

Checkpoint
What advantage does the second path have over the first?
A) It does not require proving the world's temporal origination.
B) It relies on the authority of the Quran.
C) It uses a different definition of possibility.
D) It avoids the need for a specifier.

Key Takeaway
The second path provides a minimal and elegant proof of God's existence by starting simply with the fact that something exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You now know that the mere fact of something existing proves God. But have you allowed the busyness of daily existence to numb you to this foundational truth? When was the last time you paused and said, 'Something exists, therefore God exists' and let that sink into your heart? What will you do today to restore that awareness?
?
This path bypasses complex arguments and appeals to your innate disposition (fiṭra). Are you trusting that inner witness, or have you allowed doubts from intellectual debates to silence it? What would it take for you to listen to that simple voice within you again?
?
If the proof is so simple, why do you still sometimes feel distant from God? Is it because you are looking for Him in complicated theories instead of seeing Him in the very fact of your own existence? Commit today to start each morning with the thought: 'I exist, and that points to His existence.' How will you hold yourself to that commitment?

The Third Path: Composite Beings and the Necessary Cause
Sets forth the third path for proving the Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the possibility of composite beings leads to the necessity of an external cause, even if the chain of possibles is infinite.

Core Idea
An infinite chain of possibles is itself possible and needs a cause.

After exploring standard cosmological arguments, we now examine a refined version attributed to later scholars like Suhrawardī. This 'third path' avoids directly proving the impossibility of infinite regress. Instead, it treats the entire infinite chain of possible beings as a single possible entity that itself requires a cause. Let's see how this argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation without analysis. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
Pay attention to the phrase 'من حيث هو جميع ممكن'.
2
Identify the two starting options
The argument begins by noting that a possible being either is based on the Necessary directly or ultimately leads to It. If not, we have an infinite chain.
3
Understand the key inference
The passage argues that the entire infinite chain of possibles is itself possible because it depends on its parts, which are other than itself. Therefore, it needs an existing cause beyond it.
This is the crux of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الثالث : لبعض المتأخرين يعني صاحب التلويحات وهو أنه لا شك في وجود ممكن كالمركبات فإن استند إلى الواجب ابتداء أو انتهى إليه فذاك، وإن تسلسلت الممكنات قلنا ( جميع الممكنات ( المتسلسلة إلى غير النهاية، ( من حيث هو جميع ممكن لاحتياجه إلى أجزائه التي هي غيره فله علة موجودة
The third path: For some later scholars, meaning the author of al-Talwīḥāt, and it is that there is no doubt about the existence of a possible being, such as composites. So if it is based on the necessary from the beginning or ends with it, then that is it. And if the possible beings are sequenced ad infinitum, we say: all the possible beings that are sequenced ad infinitum, insofar as they are all possible because of their need for their parts which are other than themselves, have an existing cause.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage presents a third path for proving the existence of God, attributed to later scholars, specifically the author of al-Talwīḥāt (likely Suhrawardī). The argument begins by asserting that there is no doubt about the existence of possible beings (mumkināt), such as composite things (murakkabāt). If such a possible being is directly caused by the necessary being (wājib) from the start, or ultimately ends with the necessary, then the conclusion is reached. But if the possible beings form an infinite chain (tasalsul), we argue: consider the entire chain of possible beings extending ad infinitum; insofar as it is a collection of possibles (mumkin), it depends on its parts, which are other than itself. Therefore, the whole chain requires an existing cause ('illa mawjūda) that is external to it, which must be the necessary being. This argument avoids the need to prove the impossibility of infinite regress directly; instead, it shows that the infinite chain itself, being dependent on its parts, is possible and thus requires a cause. The author presents this as an alternative to the standard cosmological arguments.

Key Terms

المتأخرين
صاحب التلويحات
ممكن
مركبات
الواجب
تسلسل
علة موجودة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument requires proving that infinite regress is impossible.
Correction: The argument does not require proving impossibility of regress; it treats the regress itself as a possible whole that needs a cause.

Checkpoint
According to the third path, why does an infinite chain of possible beings require an external cause?
A) Because each individual is possible.
B) Because the whole chain, being dependent on its parts, is possible.
C) Because an infinite regress is impossible.
D) Because composites are inherently unstable.

Key Takeaway
The third path proves the Necessary Being by showing that even an infinite chain of possibles is itself a possible whole requiring a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are a composite of body and soul, both contingent and dependent on God. Do you live each moment aware that your existence is sustained by the Necessary Being, or do you take it for granted? What one habit would change if you truly internalized this dependence?
?
The argument considers an infinite chain of possibles as a whole needing a cause. Think of the endless stream of causes in your life—your lineage, decisions, events. Even if that chain were infinite, your very being still demands a Creator. How does this realization affect your sense of purpose? Will you thank God today for holding you in existence?
?
This path was developed by later scholars to strengthen the proof, but your faith does not rest solely on arguments. In what area of your life do you rely more on intellectual certainty than on heartfelt submission? Will you ask God to increase both your knowledge and your reliance on Him?

Second Maslak: Philosophers' Proof from Existence
The second proof from the philosophers (hukamā') establishing the necessary existent by considering existence in general.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' simplified proof for the necessary existent based on the mere existence of something in reality, and how it avoids extensive prior arguments.

Core Idea
The simplest proof for God starts with: something exists, therefore a Necessary Being exists.

The philosophers offer a leaner, more elegant argument than the first maslak. Instead of proving the world’s temporality and possibility, they start from the self-evident fact that something exists. From there, a simple dichotomy leads directly to a Necessary Being. This lesson unpacks that proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Let the philosophers' own formulation sink in.
The passage is concise; note the phrase 'disregarding particularities'.
2
Identify the starting premise
Find the premise that 'in reality, an existent exists' abstracted from all details. This is claimed to be self-evident (fitra).
3
Follow the dichotomy
The passage then splits into two branches: if that existent is necessary, we are done; if possible, it needs a cause.
Focus on the logical move from possibility to need for an effect (mu'aththir).
4
Understand the termination requirement
If the existent is possible, its cause chain must end in a necessary being, otherwise circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul) results.
This is a classic argument against infinite regress of dependents.
5
Notice the avoided burdens
Compare this proof to the first maslak: here no need to prove the world's temporality or possibility; those complexities are bypassed.
The passage says 'as you see' – appreciate the elegance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الثاني : للحكماء وهو أن ) في الواقع (موجوداً) مع قطع النظر عن خصوصيات الموجودات وأحوالها وهذه مقدمة يشهد بها كل ،فطرة ( فإن كان ذلك الموجود ) واجباً فذاك ) هو المطلوب ( وإن كان ممكناً احتاج إلى مؤثر ولا بد من الانتهاء إلى الواجب وإلا لزم الدور أو التسلسل وفي هذا المسلك ( طرح لمؤنات كثيرة ) كانت في المسلك الأول من بيان حدوث العالم وإمكانه وما يتوجه عليه من الأسئلة والأجوبة عنها فإنها سقطت هاهنا ( كما ترى .
The second approach (maslak) of the philosophers: that in reality an existent exists, disregarding particularities of existents and their states, and this is a premise witnessed by every innate disposition. Then if that existent is necessary, that is what is sought. And if it is possible, it needs an effect, and one must end at the necessary, otherwise circularity or infinite regress would follow. And in this approach, many burdens are cast off that were in the first approach concerning proving the world's temporality and its possibility and the questions and answers directed at it; for they have fallen away here, as you see.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof (maslak) of the philosophers (al-ḥukamāʼ) for establishing the existence of a necessary being (wājib). It begins by positing that there exists something in reality (موجود في الواقع), abstracting from all particularities and states of individual existents. This premise is considered self-evident to every sound innate disposition (fiṭra). Then it applies a dichotomy: if that existent is necessary (wājib), the goal is achieved; if it is possible (mumkin), it requires an effect (muʼaththir) and must ultimately terminate in a necessary being, otherwise circularity or infinite regress would ensue. The passage notes that this second approach avoids many of the burdens of the first approach, such as proving the world's temporality and its possibility, and the associated objections and answers. This is a cleaner, more elegant proof.

مؤثر
دور
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this proof does not require establishing the existence of a creator.
Correction: The proof directly leads to a necessary being as the ultimate cause; it does not avoid the need for a creator.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' proof, if an existent is possible, what must happen?
A) It remains uncaused
B) It leads to circularity
C) It must be referred to a necessary being
D) It becomes necessary

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' second proof uses the self-evident existence of something to directly conclude a Necessary Being, bypassing many preparatory arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your own existence is merely possible, how do you live as if you are self-sufficient? Are you relying on the Necessary Being every moment, or have you forgotten that you need a Sustainer?
?
The proof begins with 'something exists.' Look around you right now. What one thing in your sight screams that it could not have caused itself? How does that change your sense of awe and dependence on the Creator?
?
If the philosophers' proof bypasses many arguments, does your heart accept that the simplest evidence is enough? Or do you demand more signs? What does that say about your inner certainty?

Third Proof: The Totality of Possible Beings
A proof using the totality of an infinite regress of possible beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a third proof for the existence of God based on the contingency of a totality of possible beings in an infinite regress.

Core Idea
Even an infinite chain of possible beings needs a cause outside itself.

This lesson presents a third approach (maslak) to proving the Necessary Being, attributed to later thinkers like the author of al-Talwīḥāt. The proof considers the possibility of an infinite regress of contingent beings and argues that the entire totality, being composed of parts, requires an external cause. Understanding this argument deepens one's grasp of how contingency points to a necessary creator.

Guided Path
1
Context of the third proof
Learn who proposed this proof and why it is considered a distinct approach from earlier proofs.
2
Trace the argument structure
Follow the two cases: if possible being depends on Necessary directly, done; if infinite regress, focus on the totality.
3
Understand why the totality needs a cause
Grasp that the totality is contingent because it depends on its parts, so it requires an external cause.
Reflect on the final remark: the argument ultimately returns to possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الثالث : لبعض المتأخرين يعني صاحب التلويحات وهو أنه لا شك في وجود ممكن كالمركبات فإن استند إلى الواجب ابتداء أو انتهى إليه فذاك، وإن تسلسلت الممكنات قلنا ( جميع الممكنات ( المتسلسلة إلى غير النهاية، ( من حيث هو جميع ممكن لاحتياجه إلى أجزائه التي هي غيره فله علة موجودة لانتقاضه بالصفات، بل لاستلزامه الإمكان كما سيأتي فالرجوع بالآخرة إلى الإمكان وحده حينئذ أيضاً فليتأمل.
The third approach of some later thinkers, meaning the author of al-Talwīḥāt: that there is no doubt about the existence of something possible, like compounds. If it depends on the necessary from the beginning or ends at it, that is it. And if possible beings are arranged in infinite regress, we say: all the possible beings in that infinite regress, insofar as it is a totality of possible things, due to its need for its parts which are other than it, has an existing cause. Because of its invalidation by attributes, rather it necessitates possibility as will come, so the return ultimately is to possibility alone, then also consider.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof (maslak) attributed to later thinkers, specifically the author of al-Talwīḥāt (Suhrawardī or similar). The argument begins by acknowledging the existence of a possible being (mumkin), such as a compound (murakkab). If that possible being is directly dependent on the necessary (wājib) from the start or ultimately ends at it, the goal is achieved. However, if possible beings are arranged in an infinite regress (tasalsul), the entire set of possible beings forming an infinite series is considered as a totality (jumla). This totality, being composed of parts that are other than itself, requires a cause that is not part of it. Because the totality is contingent (imkān) due to its need for parts, it cannot be self-caused. Therefore, its cause must be external to all possible beings, and that external cause is necessarily the necessary being. The passage ends with a remark that the argument ultimately returns to the concept of possibility alone, urging careful reflection (fa-l-yataʼammal).

صاحب التلويحات
ممكن
مركبات
المجموع
علة
إمكان
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The totality of an infinite regress of possible beings is self-sufficient and needs no cause.
Correction: The totality is composed of parts and is therefore contingent, requiring an external cause.

Checkpoint
What is the key point of the third proof regarding the totality of possible beings?
A) The totality is self-sufficient
B) The totality needs a cause outside itself
C) The totality is necessary
D) The totality is impossible

Key Takeaway
The totality of an infinite regress of possible beings, being composed of parts, is contingent and requires an external cause, which is the Necessary Being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that even an infinite chain of possible beings requires a cause outside itself. You, as a contingent being, rely on something beyond yourself for every moment of your existence. Do you live with this dependence at the center of your consciousness, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? What would change today if you truly internalized that you are sustained by the Necessary Being from moment to moment?
?
The totality of possible beings is contingent because it needs its parts. Consider your own life as a 'totality' of your actions, relationships, and achievements. In what ways do you treat your own 'totality' as if it explains itself—needing no external purpose or sustainer? What part of your life are you most tempted to think is self-sufficient, and how can you explicitly redirect that to the Necessary Being?
?
If the infinite regress of possibilities cannot be the ultimate explanation, then your own attempts to justify your existence by looking to finite causes (your job, your family, your legacy) are ultimately inadequate. What 'infinite regress' of worldly supports are you relying on to give your life meaning? How does this lesson call you to stop that regress today and anchor your identity in the Necessary Being?
