خلف
دور
تسلسل
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fourth proof is flawed because it is circular.
Correction: The circularity is intentional as a reductio ad absurdum; the argument shows that denying the Necessary Being leads to a contradiction, which is a valid proof.

Checkpoint
What is the response to the objection that the fourth maslak is circular?
A) The objection is invalid because circularity is impossible.
B) The circularity is the very thing desired, so it is a valid proof.
C) The proof avoids circularity by assuming infinite regress.
D) The proof does not need to address the objection.

Key Takeaway
The fourth maslak proves the Necessary Being through a reductio that does not rely on refuting infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that everything around you is merely possible and could cease to exist at any moment, would you still take your comfort and security for granted? The proof reveals that only the Necessary Being is truly self-sufficient. Are you living as if your existence depends on Him, or as if you are self-sufficient? What would it look like to rely on Him for every breath?
?
The objection raised against this proof is that it is circular. But the response shows that the circularity is precisely the point—it forces you to see that denying the Necessary Being leads to absurdity. When you face inner doubts about Allah's existence, do you treat them as intellectual puzzles to be solved, or do you let the logical necessity pierce your heart? Which doubts have you been holding onto that this reductio should have already demolished?
?
The proof does not depend on long chains of cause and effect; it simply looks at the reality that everything external to possibles must be necessary. Can you, in this moment, look at the world around you and see the utter contingency of everything? Or do you get lost in secondary causes? How can you train your mind to see the 'external'—the direct hand of the Necessary Being—in every moment of your life?

The Fifth Proof: Impossibility of a World of Only Possibles
Sanusi presents the fifth proof in two parts and notes its closeness to the fourth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth proof for the Necessary Being, which shows that a universe composed solely of possibles would be susceptible to total non-existence, and how this proof parallels the fourth proof.

Core Idea
If all existents are merely possible, their total non-existence is not impossible—only a Necessary Being prevents it.

The fifth proof for the Necessary Being builds on the idea that a universe of only possibles could simply cease to exist. In this passage, Sanusi explains why the complete removal of all possible things is not impossible unless a Necessary Being exists. This proof is closely related to the fourth, but approaches from the angle of impossibility of non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse every detail. Then read the translation to get the overall flow of the two-part argument.
Notice how Sanusi uses a compressed, logical style.
2
Understand the first part
Focus on the first part: why does the non-existence of a Necessary Being imply that no possible existent exists? The text argues that if there is no Necessary Being, all existents are possible, and their complete removal is not impossible.
The key point: the non-removal of possible things is only guaranteed by a Necessary Being.
3
Understand the second part
Now examine the second part: why does the total absence of any necessary being (neither in itself nor by another) lead to nothing existing? The reasoning is that everything that exists must be necessary in some sense.
This part reinforces the conclusion of the first part.
4
Appreciate the connection to the fourth proof
Read the commentary on how this proof is close to the fourth but focuses on impossibility of non-existence rather than necessity of existence. Notice that both proofs avoid circularity and infinite regress.
This relationship helps in understanding the broader structure of kalam arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( المسلك الخامس : ( وهو قريب مما قبله لو لم يوجد واجب لذاته لم يوجد واجب لغيره أي ممكن وحينئذ ( فيلزم أن لا يوجد موجود ) أصلاً ضرورة إنحصار الموجود في الواجب والممكن ( أما الأول) وهو أنه إذا لم يوجد واجب لم يوجد ممكن (فلان) الواجب إذا لم يوجد كانت الموجودات بأسرها ممكنة، ولا شك أن ارتفاع (الجميع المركب من الممكنات فقط ( مرة ) ( أي بالكلية ( لا يكون ) على ذلك التقدير ( ممتنعاً لا بالذات )، وهو ظاهر لأنه وآحاده برمتها ممكنة ) ولا بالغير لما عرفت من أن الغير الذي يمتنع به رفع الجميع بالمرة لا بد أن يكون موجوداً خارجاً عنه واجباً لذاته والمفروض عدمه، ( وأما الثاني ) وهو أنه إذا لم يوجد واجب بذاته ولا بغيره لم يوجد موجود أصلا ( فلأن ما لم يجـ بالذات وإما بالغير لا يوجد كما تقدم من أن الموجود إما واجب مسبوق وجوده بوجوبه الذاتي، وإما ممكن مسبوق وجوده بوجوبه من علته، وهذا المسلك كالرابع في الإستغناء عن حديث الدور والتسلسل وقربه منه مكشوف لا سترة به قوله : ( وقربه منه مكشوف فإن الرابع ناظر إلى وجوب الوجود والخامس ناظر إلى امتناع العدم، وهما متقاربان كذا نقل من الشارح ولك أن تعكس حديث النظر كما يشعر به قول الشارح في المسلك الرابع ومستخرج من ملاحظة حال عدم المعلول إلخ .
The fifth path: It is close to the previous: if a Necessary Being does not exist in itself, then a necessary being by another—i.e., a possible—does not exist, and then it follows that no existent exists at all, necessarily because existents are confined to the necessary and the possible. As for the first: that if a necessary being does not exist, a possible does not exist, because if the necessary being does not exist, all existents are possible, and there is no doubt that the removal of the whole composed only of possibles once—i.e., completely—is not impossible on that assumption—neither by essence, which is apparent because its individuals are entirely possible, nor by another, because you know that the other by which the whole would be impossible to be removed completely must be an existent external to it, necessary in itself, and it is assumed non-existent. As for the second: that if no necessary being exists either in itself or by another, no existent exists at all, because whatever is not necessary in itself is necessary by another and does not exist, as previously explained that the existent is either necessary, its existence preceded by its essential necessity, or possible, its existence preceded by its necessity from its cause. And this path is like the fourth in dispensing with the discussion of circularity and infinite regress, and its closeness to it is obvious, no veil over it. His saying: “And its closeness to it is obvious,” for the fourth looks at the necessity of existence and the fifth looks at the impossibility of non-existence, and they are close. Thus transmitted from the commentator, but you may invert the consideration as suggested by the commentator’s statement in the fourth path: “and it is extracted from considering the state of the non-existence of the effect in relation to its cause,” etc.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof for the Necessary Being, which is close to the fourth. The proof has two parts: First, if there is no Necessary Being in itself, then there is no necessary being by another (i.e., a possible). Why? Because if the Necessary Being did not exist, all would be possible. The complete non-existence of all possibles is not impossible—neither by itself (since each possible can cease) nor by an external factor (since that external would have to be necessary and it is absent). Therefore, it is possible for all possibles to cease completely, so no possible would exist. Second, if no necessary being exists at all (neither in itself nor by another), then nothing exists because everything that exists is either necessary in itself or necessary by another (i.e., caused). The proof does not need to address circularity or infinite regress. The marginal commentary notes that the fourth path focuses on necessary existence, while the fifth focuses on the impossibility of non-existence, but they are essentially close.

Key Terms
واجب لذاته
واجب لغيره
ممكن
ارتفاع الجميع
امتناع العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fifth proof assumes that the non-existence of all possibles is possible, but it is actually a conclusion drawn from the absence of a Necessary Being.
Correction: The proof does not assume; it argues that if all are possible, their total removal is not impossible because no Necessary Being exists to prevent it. It is a logical necessity, not an assumption.

Checkpoint
Why, according to the fifth maslak, does the non-existence of all possibles not become impossible if no Necessary Being exists?
A) Because possibles are necessary by themselves.
B) Because there is no external necessary being to make their removal impossible.
C) Because infinite regress makes it impossible.
D) Because possibles cannot cease to exist.

Key Takeaway
The fifth proof shows that without a Necessary Being, the complete non-existence of all possibles is not impossible, so nothing would exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that without Allah nothing can exist for even a moment, would your attitude towards daily struggles—like earning a living or maintaining health—change? What specific action can you take today to act on that belief?
?
The proof argues that if all things were only possible, their total non-existence would be possible. Does your heart truly rely on Allah's necessity as the only guarantee of your existence? When you face difficulties, do you act as if your existence depends on Allah alone, or do you rely on other things as if they are necessary?
?
Consider a time you felt self-sufficient or thought you could manage without Allah's constant support. How does the fifth proof challenge that feeling? What would it mean for your prayer and reliance (tawakkul) if you internalized that every moment you exist is because Allah, the Necessary Being, makes it so?

The Sixth Proof: The Shortest Path to the Necessary Being
Presents the sixth maslak for the Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the sixth maslak and why it is considered the shortest proof for the Necessary Being.

Core Idea
The possible cannot cause existence without first existing, so only a Necessary Being can initiate the chain.

Sanusi presents the sixth proof, which some scholars consider the most concise argument for the Necessary Being. It focuses on the impossibility of possible beings independently causing existence. This lesson unpacks the proof and its key premise.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the concise wording.
2
Identify the two negations
The proof denies that a possible being can be independent in its own existence and in causing existence for another. Find these two points.
This is the core of the argument.
3
Understand the conclusion
If all beings were possible, none could cause existence, so nothing would exist. Since things do exist, there must be a Necessary Being.
Contrast with the idea of independent causation.
4
Note Sanusi's claim
Sanusi states that this is the shortest and most apparent of the paths. Think about why it might be considered so.
Requires only one key premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( المسلك السادس ما أشار إليه بعض الفضلاء وتحريره أن الممكن لا يستقل بنفسه في وجوده وهو ظاهر، ولا في إيجاده لغيره لأن مرتبة الإيجاد بعد مرتبة الوجود فإن الشيء ما لم يوجد لم يوجد فلو انحصر الممكن لزم أن لا يوجد شيء أصلاً لأن الممكن وإن كان متعدداً لا يستقل بوجود ولا إيجاد، وإذ لا وجود ولا إيجاد فلا موجود لا بذاته ولا بغيره، وهذا المسلك أخصر المسلاك وأظهرها قوله : ( لزم أن لا يوجد شيء أصلاً إلخ) قيل هذا المسلك أيضاً محتاج إلى إبطال التسلسل لأن كل ممكن وإن لم يستحق الوجود نظراً إلى نفسه استحقه نظراً إلى علته فيجوز أن يعلل كل من الممكنات بعلة ممكنة لا إلى نهاية فيستحق الوجود والإيجاد بالنظر إليها، والظاهر أن هذا المسلك يحتاج إلى نوع حدس . قوله : ( وهذا المسلك أخصر المسالك وأظهرها قيل هاهنا مسلك آخر لطيف وهو لم يوجد الواجب لا نحصر الموجود في الممكن، وكل ممكن موجود لا بدله من علة تامة بالضرورة فإن كانت علة ذلك الموجود المفروض ممكنة احتاجت أيضا إلى علة فلا يكون نفسها علة تامة للممكن المفروض أولا، لاحتياجه إلى علة تلك العلة أيضاً وإن كانت واجبة أو مشتملة عليه ثبت المطلوب وفيه أنّ العلة التامة للممكن في مرتبة من المراتب جاز أن يكون نفسه علتها كما أشار إليه في الاعتراض على المسلك الثالث، وأما فاعله المستقل بالتأثير فجاز أن يكون جزاً منها وبالجملة بعض الاعتراضات الموردة على المسالك المتقدمة متأت هاهنا أيضاً، فتأمل.
The sixth path: that which some virtuosi indicated, and its formulation is that the possible is not independent by itself in its existence—that is apparent—nor in causing existence for another, because the rank of causing existence comes after the rank of existence, for a thing as long as it has not existed does not cause existence. So if the possible alone is confined, then it would follow that nothing exists at all, because the possible, even if multiple, is not independent in existence or causation. And when there is no existence and no causation, then there is no existent either in itself or by another. And this path is the shortest and most apparent of the paths. His saying: “then it would follow that nothing exists at all, etc.” It is said: this path also needs to refute infinite regress, because every possible, even if it does not deserve existence by itself, deserves it by its cause, so it is possible that each possible is caused by another possible ad infinitum, thus deserving existence and causation by them. And it appears that this path requires a kind of intuition. His saying: “And this path is the shortest and most apparent of the paths.” It is said: there is another subtle path here, which is: if the Necessary Being did not exist, existents would be confined to the possible, and every possible existent must have a complete cause necessarily. If the cause of that supposed existent is possible, it also needs a cause, so it is not itself a complete cause for the supposed possible in the first place, because it needs the cause of that cause as well. If it is necessary or includes it, the desired is proven. But it contains that the complete cause of a possible in a certain rank may be its own cause, as was indicated in the objection against the third path, and its independent agent of causation may be a part of it. In short, some of the objections raised against the previous paths are also relevant here, so ponder.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof for the Necessary Being, which is said to be the shortest and most apparent. The proof argues that any possible being cannot be independent in its own existence or in causing existence for another. Since causation presupposes existence, a possible cannot cause anything until it exists. If all beings were only possibles, no being could initiate existence or causation, so nothing would exist at all. The first critical commentary notes that this proof may still need to refute infinite regress, because a possible can derive its existence from another possible in an infinite chain, thus deserving existence and causation through that chain. The second commentary suggests another subtle path: if the Necessary Being does not exist, all existents are possible. Every possible needs a complete cause. If that cause is possible, it needs another cause, so the first cause is not truly complete. If the cause is necessary or includes a necessary, the desired is proven. However, the commentator notes that a possible's complete cause could be its own cause at a different rank, as in the objection against the third maslak, and the independent agent may be part of it. The passage concludes that some objections from previous paths apply here as well.

Key Terms
ممكن
إيجاد
استقلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key premise of the sixth maslak?
A) Possible beings can cause existence but not sustain it.
B) Possible beings cannot cause existence because they need to exist first.
C) Possible beings are independent in existence but not in causation.
D) The shortest proof is always the best.

Key Takeaway
The sixth maslak proves the Necessary Being by showing that without it, nothing would exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the possible cannot cause existence, then your very existence depends entirely on the Necessary Being. Do you truly live as if your existence is dependent on Him at every moment? What would change if you remembered this dependence before every action?
?
This proof shows that without the Necessary Being, nothing would exist. Are there areas in your life where you act as if you are self-sufficient, forgetting that your existence is borrowed? What would it mean to live in constant awareness of your contingency?
?
If you truly believed that you are not independent in causing anything, how would that affect your pride, your sense of control, your reliance on others? What is one concrete step you can take today to acknowledge your complete dependence on Allah?

Critiques of the Sixth Proof and a Subtle Alternative
Presents two critical commentaries on the sixth proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main criticisms of the sixth proof and the alternative subtle path proposed, along with its counter-objection.

Core Idea
Even the shortest proof faces objections about infinite regress and the completeness of causation.

While the sixth proof seems concise, scholars have pointed out potential flaws. This lesson examines the first critique regarding infinite regress, and a second subtle path that attempts to reformulate the argument, along with its rebuttal. These discussions show the depth of kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the first criticism
Read the passage from 'قيل هذا المسلك أيضاً محتاج إلى إبطال التسلسل' to 'يحتاج إلى نوع حدس'. Understand the criticism that the proof implicitly requires refuting infinite regress.
The critic argues that possibles can derive existence from an infinite chain.
2
Read the second subtle path
Read the alternative argument starting from 'وكل ممكن موجود لا بد له من علة تامة'. Notice how it tries to avoid the regress issue.
This path uses the concept of a complete cause.
3
Understand the counter-objection
Read the rebuttal beginning with 'وفيه'. The commentator points out that the complete cause could be the thing itself at a different rank, echoing earlier objections.
This refers back to the third maslak.
4
Note the concluding remark
Sanusi concludes that many previous objections apply here as well. Reflect on how this shows the interconnectedness of the proofs.
Students are encouraged to ponder (فتأمل).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( المسلك السادس ما أشار إليه بعض الفضلاء وتحريره أن الممكن لا يستقل بنفسه في وجوده وهو ظاهر، ولا في إيجاده لغيره لأن مرتبة الإيجاد بعد مرتبة الوجود فإن الشيء ما لم يوجد لم يوجد فلو انحصر الممكن لزم أن لا يوجد شيء أصلاً لأن الممكن وإن كان متعدداً لا يستقل بوجود ولا إيجاد، وإذ لا وجود ولا إيجاد فلا موجود لا بذاته ولا بغيره، وهذا المسلك أخصر المسلاك وأظهرها قوله : ( لزم أن لا يوجد شيء أصلاً إلخ) قيل هذا المسلك أيضاً محتاج إلى إبطال التسلسل لأن كل ممكن وإن لم يستحق الوجود نظراً إلى نفسه استحقه نظراً إلى علته فيجوز أن يعلل كل من الممكنات بعلة ممكنة لا إلى نهاية فيستحق الوجود والإيجاد بالنظر إليها، والظاهر أن هذا المسلك يحتاج إلى نوع حدس . قوله : ( وهذا المسلك أخصر المسالك وأظهرها قيل هاهنا مسلك آخر لطيف وهو لم يوجد الواجب لا نحصر الموجود في الممكن، وكل ممكن موجود لا بدله من علة تامة بالضرورة فإن كانت علة ذلك الموجود المفروض ممكنة احتاجت أيضا إلى علة فلا يكون نفسها علة تامة للممكن المفروض أولا، لاحتياجه إلى علة تلك العلة أيضاً وإن كانت واجبة أو مشتملة عليه ثبت المطلوب وفيه أنّ العلة التامة للممكن في مرتبة من المراتب جاز أن يكون نفسه علتها كما أشار إليه في الاعتراض على المسلك الثالث، وأما فاعله المستقل بالتأثير فجاز أن يكون جزاً منها وبالجملة بعض الاعتراضات الموردة على المسالك المتقدمة متأت هاهنا أيضاً، فتأمل.
The sixth path: that which some virtuosi indicated, and its formulation is that the possible is not independent by itself in its existence—that is apparent—nor in causing existence for another, because the rank of causing existence comes after the rank of existence, for a thing as long as it has not existed does not cause existence. So if the possible alone is confined, then it would follow that nothing exists at all, because the possible, even if multiple, is not independent in existence or causation. And when there is no existence and no causation, then there is no existent either in itself or by another. And this path is the shortest and most apparent of the paths. His saying: “then it would follow that nothing exists at all, etc.” It is said: this path also needs to refute infinite regress, because every possible, even if it does not deserve existence by itself, deserves it by its cause, so it is possible that each possible is caused by another possible ad infinitum, thus deserving existence and causation by them. And it appears that this path requires a kind of intuition. His saying: “And this path is the shortest and most apparent of the paths.” It is said: there is another subtle path here, which is: if the Necessary Being did not exist, existents would be confined to the possible, and every possible existent must have a complete cause necessarily. If the cause of that supposed existent is possible, it also needs a cause, so it is not itself a complete cause for the supposed possible in the first place, because it needs the cause of that cause as well. If it is necessary or includes it, the desired is proven. But it contains that the complete cause of a possible in a certain rank may be its own cause, as was indicated in the objection against the third path, and its independent agent of causation may be a part of it. In short, some of the objections raised against the previous paths are also relevant here, so ponder.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof for the Necessary Being, which is said to be the shortest and most apparent. The proof argues that any possible being cannot be independent in its own existence or in causing existence for another. Since causation presupposes existence, a possible cannot cause anything until it exists. If all beings were only possibles, no being could initiate existence or causation, so nothing would exist at all. The first critical commentary notes that this proof may still need to refute infinite regress, because a possible can derive its existence from another possible in an infinite chain, thus deserving existence and causation through that chain. The second commentary suggests another subtle path: if the Necessary Being does not exist, all existents are possible. Every possible needs a complete cause. If that cause is possible, it needs another cause, so the first cause is not truly complete. If the cause is necessary or includes a necessary, the desired is proven. However, the commentator notes that a possible's complete cause could be its own cause at a different rank, as in the objection against the third maslak, and the independent agent may be part of it. The passage concludes that some objections from previous paths apply here as well.

علة تامة
حدس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sixth maslak is completely independent of the need to refute infinite regress.
Correction: The first critique shows that the proof may still require rejecting infinite regress, as possibles could derive existence through an infinite chain.

Checkpoint
What is the main difficulty raised against the alternative subtle path?
A) It relies on intuition.
B) It assumes that the complete cause cannot be the thing itself at a different rank.
C) It is too long.
D) It only works for some possibles.

Key Takeaway
Even the shortest proof for the Necessary Being faces serious objections, especially concerning infinite regress and the nature of complete causation.

?
The counter-objection suggests that a complete cause could include itself. Is your belief in Allah grounded in something deeper than circular reasoning? How would you know if it were?
?

The Sixth Method for Proving the Necessary Being
The author presents the sixth method's argument in a compact syllogistic form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth method's argument that contingent beings cannot exist or cause without a Necessary Being.

Core Idea
The contingent cannot stand alone; therefore, a Necessary Being must exist.

This lesson presents a concise proof for the existence of the Necessary Being. The argument is based on the observation that contingent things cannot sustain themselves or cause anything, leading to the conclusion that only a Necessary Being can break the chain. This method is praised as the shortest and most apparent.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text of the sixth method carefully. Notice the logical flow.
The passage is a single argument with a clear structure.
2
Understand the key premise
Identify the statement that contingent things cannot independently subsist in existence or bring about others. Why is this premise important?
This leads to the collapse of contingent-only existence.
3
Follow the logical consequence
If only contingents existed, nothing would exist at all. See how the argument forces the existence of a Necessary Being.
The method reduces the opponent's position to absurdity.
4
Reflect on the claim of superiority
The author claims this method is the shortest and most apparent. Consider why this might be so.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك السادس ما أشار إليه بعض الفضلاء وتحريره أن الممكن لا يستقل بنفسه في وجوده وهو ظاهر، ولا في إيجاده لغيره لأن مرتبة الإيجاد بعد مرتبة الوجود فإن الشيء ما لم يوجد لم يوجد فلو انحصر الممكن لزم أن لا يوجد شيء أصلاً لأن الممكن وإن كان متعدداً لا يستقل بوجود ولا إيجاد، وإذ لا وجود ولا إيجاد فلا موجود لا بذاته ولا بغيره، وهذا المسلك أخصر المسالك وأظهرها وقد ذكر (هاهنا) أي في مقام إثبات الصانع شبهات كثيرة) أوردها الإمام الرازي في كتبه وأجاب عنها لكن ( حاصلها أن يوجد هاهنا عائد إلى أمر واحد وهو وفي كل مسألة تراد مذهبان متقابلان فیردد بينهما ترديداً مانعاً من الخلو، ثم يبطل كل واحد منهما بدليل الآخر لئلا يلزم نفي القدر المشترك، وحلها إجمالاً هو القدح في دليل الطرف الضعيف من المذهبين أو في دليلهما إن أمكن ولا استبعاد في إمكان القدح في دليلهما معا (إد قد يكون دليل الطرفين ضعيفاً ولا يلزم من بطلان دليلهما بطلانهما) حتى يلزم ارتفاع المتقابلين وذلك لأن الدليل ملزوم للمدلول وانتفاء الملزوم لا يستلزم انتفاء لازمه ( ولنذكر منها ) أي من تلك الشبه مع أجوبتها ( عدة ) لتطلع بها على أحوال نظائرها
The sixth method: what some virtuous scholars have pointed out, and its formulation is that the contingent (al-mumkin) does not independently subsist by itself in its existence – this is apparent – nor in bringing about anything else, because the rank of bringing about follows the rank of existence. For a thing, as long as it does not exist, it cannot bring about existence. So if existence were confined to the contingent, it would necessarily follow that nothing would exist at all, because the contingent, even if multiple, does not independently subsist in existence or bringing about. And since there is no existence and no bringing about, there is no existent, neither in itself nor through another. This method is the shortest and most apparent of the methods. And he mentioned here – i.e., in the context of proving the Creator – many doubts (shubahāt) that Imam al-Rāzī raised in his books and answered. But their gist is that each doubt reduces to one thing: in every issue two opposing positions are intended, so he oscillates between them in a way that prevents the absence of both, then he invalidates each one by the other's argument, lest a common term be negated. Their general solution is to impugn the argument of the weaker side of the two positions, or of both if possible, and there is no improbability in the possibility of impugning both arguments together, for the arguments of both sides may be weak, and the invalidity of their arguments does not necessitate their own invalidity, so as to necessitate the mutual elimination of the two opposites. That is because the argument (al-dalīl) is a necessary condition (malzūm) of the conclusion (al-madlūl), and the non‑existence of the necessary condition does not entail the non‑existence of its consequent. So we will mention several of these doubts along with their answers to acquaint you with the status of similar ones.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of the sixth method for proving the existence of the Necessary Being, followed by a general discussion of the doubts raised by al-Rāzī and the method for answering them. The author first states the argument: the contingent cannot independently sustain its own existence, nor can it cause anything else because causation presupposes existence. If only contingent things existed, nothing would exist at all – a clear reduction. This method is claimed to be the shortest. Then the author notes that al-Rāzī raised many doubts in his works, but they all reduce to a single pattern: in each issue, two opposed positions are considered, al-Rāzī oscillates between them to prevent both being absent, then uses each to refute the other, thereby denying the common element. The general solution is to attack the weaker argument(s) without necessarily invalidating the positions themselves, since a weak argument does not disprove its conclusion. The passage concludes by promising to present several specific doubts and answers.

الممكن
الواجب
الإيجاد
الوجود

A) Because they lack the power to create.

C) Because they are too numerous to act together.
D) Because they are dependent on a cause for their own existence.

Key Takeaway
The sixth method proves the Necessary Being by showing that contingent beings cannot sustain themselves or cause anything, so the very fact of existence requires a self-sufficient Necessary Being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The proof shows that without a Necessary Being, nothing would exist at all. When you feel anxious about your future or your provision, are you trusting in a chain of contingencies or in the One who upholds all existence? How can you shift your reliance from created causes to the Creator?
?
This method is called the shortest and most evident. Have you ever considered that the very fact you exist is the clearest proof of Allah's existence? If you dismiss this argument, what is your heart really avoiding? Ask yourself: am I willing to accept the conclusion and submit to the One whose existence is necessary?

Pattern of Doubts and Their General Solution
The author explains the common pattern of al-Rāzī's doubts and offers a general method for answering them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Rāzī's method of raising doubts by opposing two positions and then invalidating both, and the general solution of impugning the weaker argument without rejecting the position itself.

Core Idea
Al-Rāzī's doubts set up stalemates; the answer is to attack the evidence, not the conclusion.

Imam al-Rāzī raised many doubts against theological proofs, but they all share a common pattern: he contrasts two opposing views, tries to eliminate both, and then invalidates each using the other's argument. The general solution is to critique the arguments themselves, not necessarily the positions they support. This lesson explains that pattern and prepares you for specific examples to follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage describing the pattern
Read the Arabic text that explains how al-Rāzī constructs his doubts. Pay attention to the phrase 'في كل مسألة تراد مذهبان متقابلان...'
This describes the structure of the doubt.
2
Understand the oscillation technique
Al-Rāzī oscillates between two opposing positions to prevent both being false. Then he uses each to refute the other.
This aims to deny the common subject (al-qadr al-mushtarak).
3
Learn the general solution
The solution is to attack the argument of the weaker side, or both if needed. Invalid arguments do not invalidate the position itself.
The relationship between argument and conclusion is not one of necessity.
4
Note the logical point about malzūm and madlūl
The argument is a necessary condition for the conclusion, but negating the condition does not negate the conclusion.
This protects the positions from false refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك السادس ما أشار إليه بعض الفضلاء وتحريره أن الممكن لا يستقل بنفسه في وجوده وهو ظاهر، ولا في إيجاده لغيره لأن مرتبة الإيجاد بعد مرتبة الوجود فإن الشيء ما لم يوجد لم يوجد فلو انحصر الممكن لزم أن لا يوجد شيء أصلاً لأن الممكن وإن كان متعدداً لا يستقل بوجود ولا إيجاد، وإذ لا وجود ولا إيجاد فلا موجود لا بذاته ولا بغيره، وهذا المسلك أخصر المسالك وأظهرها وقد ذكر (هاهنا) أي في مقام إثبات الصانع شبهات كثيرة) أوردها الإمام الرازي في كتبه وأجاب عنها لكن ( حاصلها أن يوجد هاهنا عائد إلى أمر واحد وهو وفي كل مسألة تراد مذهبان متقابلان فیردد بينهما ترديداً مانعاً من الخلو، ثم يبطل كل واحد منهما بدليل الآخر لئلا يلزم نفي القدر المشترك، وحلها إجمالاً هو القدح في دليل الطرف الضعيف من المذهبين أو في دليلهما إن أمكن ولا استبعاد في إمكان القدح في دليلهما معا (إد قد يكون دليل الطرفين ضعيفاً ولا يلزم من بطلان دليلهما بطلانهما) حتى يلزم ارتفاع المتقابلين وذلك لأن الدليل ملزوم للمدلول وانتفاء الملزوم لا يستلزم انتفاء لازمه ( ولنذكر منها ) أي من تلك الشبه مع أجوبتها ( عدة ) لتطلع بها على أحوال نظائرها
The sixth method: what some virtuous scholars have pointed out, and its formulation is that the contingent (al-mumkin) does not independently subsist by itself in its existence – this is apparent – nor in bringing about anything else, because the rank of bringing about follows the rank of existence. For a thing, as long as it does not exist, it cannot bring about existence. So if existence were confined to the contingent, it would necessarily follow that nothing would exist at all, because the contingent, even if multiple, does not independently subsist in existence or bringing about. And since there is no existence and no bringing about, there is no existent, neither in itself nor through another. This method is the shortest and most apparent of the methods. And he mentioned here – i.e., in the context of proving the Creator – many doubts (shubahāt) that Imam al-Rāzī raised in his books and answered. But their gist is that each doubt reduces to one thing: in every issue two opposing positions are intended, so he oscillates between them in a way that prevents the absence of both, then he invalidates each one by the other's argument, lest a common term be negated. Their general solution is to impugn the argument of the weaker side of the two positions, or of both if possible, and there is no improbability in the possibility of impugning both arguments together, for the arguments of both sides may be weak, and the invalidity of their arguments does not necessitate their own invalidity, so as to necessitate the mutual elimination of the two opposites. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If both arguments in a debate are shown to be invalid, then both opposing positions are false.
Correction: The invalidity of arguments does not entail the invalidity of the positions themselves, because the argument is only a necessary condition, not the position itself.

Key Takeaway
Al-Rāzī's doubts follow a pattern of opposing positions and mutual invalidation, but the way to answer them is to critique the evidence, not the beliefs themselves.

The First Doubt: Existence as Essence or Accident
First doubt on existence as essence or accident with refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first doubt about the relationship between existence and essence in the Necessary Being and how it is refuted.

Core Idea
Not all existence is common: the Necessary's existence is unique and identical to His essence.

This lesson examines a classic objection in kalam theology: if the Necessary Being exists, His existence must be either identical to His essence or an added attribute. Both options seem problematic. The response clarifies the nature of existence, distinguishing between the abstract concept and concrete reality. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping the uniqueness of the Necessary Being.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt carefully
Read the Arabic passage with translation, noticing the two alternatives presented and why each is deemed problematic.
The doubt is a dilemma.
2
Examine the answer
The answer provides two possible responses. Identify them and understand the key distinction about commonality.
3
Reflect on the key terms
Note the terms 'existence', 'essence', 'common', and 'in the external entities'. How do they clarify the issue?
This distinction is crucial for later topics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأولى : لو كان الواجب موجوداً لكان وجوده إما نفس ماهيته أو زائداً عليها ( إذ لا محال لكونه جزءاً منها ( والأول باطل لأن الوجود مشترك كما والماهية غير مشتركة، والثاني باطل وإلا كان وجوده معلول ماهيته لامتناع كونه معلولاً لغيرها ( فتتقدم ) ماهيته ( عليه ) أي على وجود ( بالوجود ) وهو محال كما سلف ) والجواب وجوده نفسه ونمنع الإشتراك ) في الوجود الذي هو عينه ( بل المشترك ) هو الوجود) بمعنى الكون في الأعيان ( أعني مفهوم الوجود العارض للموجودات الخارجية ، ( وأما ما صدق عليه الوجود (فلا) اشتراك فيه وذلك ) كالماهية والتشخص أو وجوده غيره ) أي زائد عليه ومعلول لماهيته ) وتقدم الماهية عليه ليس بالوجود كما تقدم
The first [doubt]: If the Necessary (al-wājib) were existent, then His existence would be either identical to His essence or additional to it, since it is impossible for it to be a part of it. The first is false because existence is common (mushtarak) while the essence is not common. The second is false, for otherwise His existence would be an effect of His essence, because it is impossible for it to be an effect of other than it. So His essence would precede it – i.e., precede His existence – by existence, which is impossible as previously stated. The answer: His existence is His very self, and we deny the commonality [shared character] in the existence that is identical to Him. Rather, what is common is existence in the sense of being in external entities (kawn fī al-aʿyān), i.e., the concept of existence that occurs to external existents. But as for that to which existence applies [i.e., particular existents], there is no commonality in it; it is like the essence and the individual identity. Or [the second possibility]: His existence is other than Him, i.e., additional to Him and an effect of His essence, and the precedence of the essence over it is not by existence, as has preceded.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is a doubt-and-answer concerning the relationship between existence and essence in the Necessary Being. It presents a dilemma: the existence of the Necessary must be either identical to His essence or an added attribute. The first option is rejected because existence is a common concept, while the essence is unique. The second is rejected because then essence would cause existence, requiring precedence of essence over existence, which is impossible. The response distinguishes between existence as a common concept (instance-abstract) and the concrete reality of existence as applied to particular things. The Necessary's existence is unique, not a common attribute. Alternatively, if existence is added, the precedence of essence is not by existence but by essential priority. The passage thus defends the doctrine that God's existence is identical to His essence, or at least that the dilemma does not force a contradiction.

Key Terms
الوجود
الماهية
المشترك
المعلول
الكون في الأعيان
التشخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Necessary's existence cannot be identical to His essence because existence is common, so identity would make His essence common.
Correction: The answer clarifies that commonality is only in the abstract concept of existence (مفهوم الوجود), not in the concrete existent (ما صدق عليه الوجود). The Necessary's existence is unique and not a common attribute.

Checkpoint
How does the answer to the first doubt avoid the conclusion that the Necessary's existence is either identical to or added to His essence?
A) By denying that existence is common.
B) By distinguishing between the concept of existence and its particular instantiations.
C) By asserting that the essence is prior to existence in an essential way.
D) By denying that the Necessary is existent.

Key Takeaway
The first doubt is resolved by distinguishing between the concept of existence (common) and the unique existent of the Necessary (not common).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself imagining Allah's existence as just a bigger or more powerful version of created existence? How does the lesson's distinction between the common concept of existence and the unique reality of Allah's existence challenge that? What can you do today to train your mind to remember that His existence is unlike any other?
?
When you say 'Allah exists', is it the same kind of statement as 'this table exists'? If not, how should that difference affect the way you speak about Allah and the reverence you have when mentioning His name? Commit to one concrete change in your speech today.
?
The passage shows that the first horn of the dilemma assumes that existence is common. In your own understanding, where have you assumed that Allah's attributes (like knowing, powerful) are common with human attributes? What are you going to do to purify your belief (tawḥīd) in His attributes?

The Second Doubt: Free Will vs. Determinism
The second doubt presents a dilemma and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second doubt against the Necessary Being's existence, which argues that He must be either free or determined, and the refutation that rejects the premise of an eternal world.

Core Idea
The dilemma of free will vs. determinism collapses once we deny the eternity of the world.

This lesson examines the second of several doubts presented against the existence of a Necessary Being. The objection presents a dilemma: if God exists, He must be either free (mukhtār) or necessitating (mūjib), both of which seem problematic. The refutation cuts to the root by rejecting the shared premise that the world is eternal.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
Read the opening of the passage where the doubt is framed: if the Necessary exists, He must be either free or necessitating. Recognize that this is a forced choice.
The Arabic uses 'إما مختاراً أو موجباً' — the two options.
2
Examine each horn's problem
The passage then argues both horns lead to impossibility. Identify why the first horn (free) fails: because the world is claimed to be eternal, and the eternal cannot come from a free agent. Then identify why the second horn (necessitating) fails: it leads to eternal daily events or infinite regress.
The keywords are 'قدم الحادث اليومي' (eternity of daily events) and 'التسلسل' (infinite regress).
3
See how the answer resolves it
The answer simply states 'We do not accept that the world is eternal' and reminds that its proofs have been refuted. Understand that this removes the basis for the first horn's objection (since the world is temporal, a free agent can create it) and also avoids the second horn's problem (since the world is not eternal, the sequence of events begins at creation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية من تلك الشبه ( لو كان ( الواجب ( موجوداً لكان إما مختاراً أو موجباً، والأول باطل لأن العالم قديم بدليله والقديم لا يستند إلى المختار، والثاني باطل وإلا لزم قدم الحادث اليومي أو التسلسل ) وكلاهما محال ) والجواب لا نسلم أن العالم قديم وقد مر ضعف دلائله
The second of those doubts: If the Necessary were existent, He would be either free (mukhtār) or necessitating (mūjib). The first is false, because the world is eternal according to its proof, and the eternal does not originate from a free agent. The second is false, for otherwise it would necessitate the eternity of daily events (qidam al-ḥādith al-yawmī) or infinite regress (al-tasalsul), both of which are impossible. The answer: We do not accept that the world is eternal, and the weakness of its proofs has already passed.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage presents the second doubt concerning whether the Necessary is free or necessitating. The objection argues that if He is free, then He cannot create an eternal world (since the eternal cannot be contingent), and if He is necessitating, then temporal emergence of events leads to either an eternal series of daily events or an infinite regress. The answer is simple: we reject the claim that the world is eternal; its proofs have been refuted earlier. Thus, the problem of an eternal world arising from a free agent disappears, and the determinist horn is also avoided because the world is temporal, not eternal.

Key Terms
المختار
الموجب
القديم
الحادث اليومي
التسلسل
العالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dilemma forces a choice between God being free or determined, and both options are impossible, so God cannot exist.
Correction: The answer shows that the dilemma is only a problem if the world is eternal; but the world is temporally created, so a free agent can create it, and the necessitating option leads to absurdities only if the world is eternal, which is denied.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the second doubt, why is the dilemma that God must be either free or necessitating not valid?
A) Because God is both free and necessitating.
B) Because the proofs for the eternity of the world are weak and have been refuted.
C) Because the eternal cannot exist.
D) Because daily events are not real.

Key Takeaway
The second doubt collapses when we reject the claim that the world is eternal, a claim whose proofs have already been refuted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel caught between two impossible choices in your life, do you ever assume that God must be either a certain way or not? How does this passage challenge you to re-examine the premises you bring to your understanding of God?
?
If God were a necessitating cause, your every action would be determined. But the passage says God is free. How does that truth confront the way you live—do you truly act as if your choices matter and that God responds to you freely?
?
The proof for the world's eternity is called 'weak.' What 'proofs' do you hold onto that keep you from seeing God's freedom in your life? Will you let them go and trust His will as the ultimate cause?

The Existence and Essence of the Necessary Being
Al-Jurjani examines whether the Necessary Being's existence is identical to or additional to its essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dilemma of whether the Necessary Being's existence is identical to or additional to its essence, and the two rational responses to this dilemma.

Core Idea
The Necessary Being's existence cannot be a separate addition without circularity.

This passage presents a classic theological dilemma: if God exists, is His existence the same as His essence or something added? Both options seem problematic. Al-Jurjani explains the objection and offers two sophisticated answers. Let's explore the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the dialectical structure: objection, two horns, answer.
The text uses parentheses for clarifications; don't get lost.
2
Identify the dilemma and its two horns
The objection presents a dilemma: existence is either identical to essence or additional. Both are claimed false. Understand why each horn is rejected.
First horn rejected because existence is common; essence is not. Second horn rejected because it leads to circular priority.
3
Understand the two answers
The answer offers two resolutions: either existence is identical (denying commonness in concrete reality), or existence is additional but priority is not in existence. Grasp how each avoids the objection.
The answer is not a single assertion but two alternative ways out.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأولى : لو كان الواجب موجوداً لكان وجوده إما نفس ماهيته أو زائداً عليها ) إذ لا محال لكونه جزءاً منها ( والأول باطل لأن الوجود مشترك كما والماهية غير مشتركة، والثاني باطل وإلا كان وجوده معلول ماهيته لامتناع كونه معلولاً لغيرها ( فتتقدم ) ماهيته ( عليه ) أي على وجود ( بالوجود ) وهو محال كما سلف ) والجواب وجوده نفسه ونمنع الإشتراك ) في الوجود الذي هو عينه ( بل المشترك ) هو الوجود) بمعنى الكون في الأعيان ( أعني مفهوم الوجود العارض للموجودات الخارجية ، ( وأما ما صدق عليه الوجود (فلا) اشتراك فيه وذلك ) كالماهية والتشخص أو وجوده غيره ) أي زائد عليه ومعلول لماهيته ) وتقدم الماهية عليه ليس بالوجود كما تقدم
The first: If the Necessary existed, its existence would be either identical to its essence or additional to it (since it is impossible for it to be a part of it). The first is false because existence is common [shared] while the essence is not common. The second is false because otherwise its existence would be caused by its essence, due to the impossibility of it being caused by something else (so its essence precedes it—i.e., precedes existence—in existence, which is impossible as previously mentioned). The answer: its existence is itself, and we deny commonness in the existence that is identical to it. Rather, what is common is existence in the sense of being in external entities, i.e., the concept of existence that accrues to external existents. As for that which existence is true of, there is no commonness in it, such as the essence and the individual character. Or its existence is other than it—i.e., additional to it and caused by its essence—and the essence's priority over it is not in existence, as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage presents the first of three objections (shubah) against the existence of the Necessary Being, along with its answer. The objection poses a dilemma: if the Necessary exists, its existence must be either identical to its essence or additional to it. The first horn is rejected because existence is a common concept shared by many, while essences are distinct and not shared. The second horn is rejected because if existence were additional, it would be caused by the essence, implying the essence precedes existence in existence, which is circular and impossible. The answer offers two resolutions. First, the Necessary's existence is itself its essence, and we deny that existence is common in that sense; what is common is merely the mental concept of existence, not the concrete reality. Second, if one takes existence as additional, the priority of the essence is not in existence but in some other respect, avoiding the circularity. The author thus defends the coherence of the Necessary Being's existence.

Key Terms
واجب
ماهية
وجود
اشتراك
معلول
مفهوم
عارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The answer is a single assertion that existence is identical to essence.
Correction: The answer actually offers two alternative responses: either existence is identical (denying commonness in reality) or existence is additional but the priority is not in existence.

Checkpoint
Why does the objection claim that existence cannot be additional to the essence in the Necessary Being?
A) Because existence would then be a part of the essence
B) Because it would require the essence to precede existence in existence, which is circular
C) Because existence and essence are identical by definition
D) Because existence is common while essence is not

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Being's existence is either identical to its essence (with commonness only in concept) or, if additional, the essence's priority is not existential but logical, avoiding circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's existence is identical to His essence, does that change how you see your own existence? Your existence is a gift added to your essence—are you grateful for that dependence, or do you live as if you are self-sufficient?
?
The passage shows that any addition to God's essence leads to circularity. Have you ever attributed to God a quality that made you think of Him as compound or needing parts? How does this truth call you to purify your belief (tawhid)?
?
When you reflect on the fact that existence cannot be an accident in God, are you content with a generic belief, or does it compel you to know Him through His true, simple essence? What will you do today to deepen that knowledge?

Free Will or Necessitation in the Necessary Being
Al-Jurjani presents the objection that the Necessary Being must be either free or necessitating, and refutes both.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dialectical objection against the Necessary Being based on the dilemma of free will vs. necessitation, and the answer that denies the world's eternality.

Core Idea
The dilemma of free will or necessitation is dismantled by denying the world's eternity.

This lesson examines a critical objection to the existence of the Necessary Being. The objection forces a choice between two models of causality, both claimed to be impossible. Al-Jurjani's concise answer cuts to the core by questioning the underlying assumption. Understanding this exchange is key to grasping the dialectical method of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the two horns of the dilemma.
Notice how the objection is framed as an 'if-then' conditional.
2
Analyze the first horn
The first horn says the Necessary is free, but the world is eternal, so it cannot be caused by a free agent. Reflect on why that is.
Free agency implies temporal origination for the effect.
3
Analyze the second horn
The second horn says the Necessary is necessitating, but then daily new events must be eternal or lead to infinite regress. Understand why that is impossible.
Necessitation would make every effect co-eternal with the cause.
4
Consider the answer
Al-Jurjani denies the premise that the world is eternal. Think about the significance of this move.
This shifts the debate to earlier proofs of the world's temporality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية من تلك الشبه ( لو كان ( الواجب ( موجوداً لكان إما مختاراً أو موجباً، والأول باطل لأن العالم قديم بدليله والقديم لا يستند إلى المختار، والثاني باطل وإلا لزم قدم الحادث اليومي أو التسلسل ) وكلاهما محال ) والجواب لا نسلم أن العالم قديم وقد مر ضعف دلائله
The second of those sophisms: If the Necessary existed, it would be either free or necessitating. The first is false because the world is eternal by its proof, and the eternal does not originate from the free agent. The second is false because otherwise it would necessitate the eternality of the daily-accruing events or an infinite regress, both of which are impossible. The answer: we do not concede that the world is eternal, and the weakness of its proofs has already been mentioned.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and its answer. The objection forces a dilemma: the Necessary Being must be either a free agent (mukhtar) or a necessitating cause (mujib). The first option is rejected on the grounds that the world is eternal (qadim), and an eternal effect cannot depend on a free agent because free agency implies temporal origination. The second option is rejected because if the Necessary were a necessitating cause, it would entail either that every new daily event is eternal (which is contradictory) or an infinite regress of causes. Both are impossible. The answer is simply to deny the premise that the world is eternal, referring to earlier arguments against its proofs.

Key Terms
مختار
موجب
قديم
حادث
تسلسل
اختيار
إيجاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection proves the impossibility of the Necessary Being.
Correction: The objection relies on the disputed premise that the world is eternal, which is denied by the answer.

Checkpoint
What is the key assumption in the objection that the Necessary Being cannot be free?
A) The Necessary Being is simple
B) The world is eternal
C) Free agency requires change in the agent
D) Necessitation leads to infinite regress

Key Takeaway
The objection that the Necessary Being must be either free or necessitating is answered by denying the premise that the world is eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a logical objection to your faith, do you quickly accept it or do you examine its hidden assumptions? This dilemma relied on the assumption that the world is eternal. What hidden assumptions might you be carrying that could weaken your trust in Allah's existence? Commit to identifying one such assumption today.
?
The response to this sophism is to deny the eternity of the world. Do you truly believe that the universe had a beginning, not just intellectually but in a way that shapes how you view your own life's purpose? If so, how does that affect your daily choices and your sense of accountability to the Creator?
?
The objection claimed the Necessary Being must be either free or necessitating, but the refutation shows that Allah's freedom is not limited by false premises. Are you willing to submit to divine revelation even when your limited reasoning seems to present a contradiction? Pray for humility to accept the truth when it challenges your own understanding.
